01What conduct is, and what shipped

The problem it solves. Today, when Momentum builds a batch of work, the old builder interrupts you constantly — approve this, confirm that, resolve this conflict, fix-or-defer each issue. Roughly 17 stop-and-ask moments in a single build, almost all for things you'd always wave through. That constant interruption is the pain this sprint set out to kill.

The idea. conduct builds the entire batch on its own — writes it, reviews it, fixes what's wrong, merges it — and interrupts you exactly once, at the end, with a single report (this one). With one deliberate exception: for the rare change that's genuinely dangerous — could destroy data, rewrite history, or break everything downstream — it stops mid-build and asks, because those can't be safely undone. Everything else waits for the end.

What now exists that didn't before — five capabilities:

  1. The Conductor — a new engine that runs a whole build by itself: works out which items are ready, builds each in isolation, runs the quality checks, fixes what they find, merges each item when it passes, then hands you one report.
  2. A "know when to stop" safety valve — a shared rulebook plus a small engine that sorts every problem into routine (fix silently) vs high-stakes (security, irreversible, or far-reaching), and pauses to ask you only for the dangerous-and-urgent ones.
  3. A fixer — the builder's worker gained a "fix mode": hand it a problem and it either fixes it, or — if it's high-stakes — raises it for you instead of quietly fixing it.
  4. Real code-review tooling — the old placeholder reviewer was replaced with an actual adversarial bug-hunter, run without interruptions, and the other workflows were pointed at it.
  5. Rescoped helpers — the quality-checker now reviews one item at a time (so a problem is pinned to the item that caused it), and the builder's worker stopped managing git itself (the Conductor owns all of that now).

Important caveat — this is the spine, not the finished machine. What shipped is the core of those five capabilities and the safety machinery around them. Conduct is not yet a runnable replacement for the old builder: a few load-bearing pieces are deliberately left for a follow-up slice, and until they land, conduct can't run a build on its own and can't be adopted as the default. §06 lays out exactly what's live versus still hollow — read it before you decide, because it bears directly on what approving does.

How the build itself went — and why that's the real proof. conduct doesn't exist yet, so I ran this build by hand as the Conductor, following the model these 21 items define. Across 6 rounds, every item was built, independently reviewed, auto-fixed, and merged. The review layer caught ~93 problems and fixed them automatically; 14 of those were serious enough that, left unfixed, they'd have caused real harm — those are §03. The mid-build "stop and ask" alarm never once fired on a false alarm, and fired exactly once for a genuine high-stakes call (§04) — which is exactly the calibration the design was aiming for.

02What each piece is for

Plain-language purpose of all 21 work items — enough to understand why a problem in any of them would matter. The ones that needed correcting are flagged and have their full risk story in §03. Open "Review this work item" on any row to actually review the work: how it was verified (testing first, and honest about what "verified" means here), why it's built that way with references to the governing decisions, and the real diff — so you're not signing off blind.

The blueprint

The design blueprintconduct-spec-revision-dec036

This story's job was to rewrite the conduct design spec so it actually matches the rule the team had just agreed to — a rule that loosened an earlier all-or-nothing stance. The earlier spec said two things absolutely: the human is asked to review only once, at the very end, and the system silently auto-fixes every problem it finds. The agreed-upon refinement keeps that as the default but carves out one narrow exception: a genuinely dangerous or build-breaking problem can interrupt the human mid-build. Because every other story in the build reads this spec as its blueprint, the guarantee here is that the blueprint tells the truth. Without this fix, every downstream builder would have faithfully re-implemented the old, stricter behavior — meaning a dangerous problem (say, a destructive action or a security gap) would have been quietly auto-handled or held silently until the end instead of being raised to a human in time.

Delivered: The conduct design spec now describes the agreed behavior end to end — a default single end-gate plus one narrowly-bounded mid-build escalation path, the new "raise it, don't silently fix it" handling for high-stakes findings, and a reconciliation note tracing each change back to the decisions it implements — so the rest of the build was constructed against a correct blueprint instead of an outdated one.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. This item is a specification rewrite, so the contract is a checklist of statements that must be *readable in the finished document*, not behaviors that run. The frozen contract listed eighteen required claims spanning four sections of `sprint-dev-redesign-spec.md` plus a new reconciliation note. In plain terms, the revised spec had to say all of the following. The single human end-gate at the end of the build is the *default*, and the only exception to it is a narrow, high-bar mid-flight escalation tier. That mid-flight tier is bounded to exactly two triggers — a finding may jump the queue mid-build *only* if it is irreversible-and-imminent OR build-invalidating — and no third condition may be introduced. Routine findings must still be stated to be *always* auto-fixed silently, while stakes-class findings get *raised* (surfaced as decision cards) rather than quietly patched. The fixer schema had to drop its old absolute ("legitimate findings are ALWAYS auto-fixed") and instead make disposition depend on a finding's stakes class and timing tier; it had to name three stakes classes (security/auth-isolation; irreversible/destructive, with examples like migration, delete, force-push, prod deploy; high-blast-radius/architecture) plus a default "routine" class, add a new `escalated` disposition alongside `fixed`/`dismissed`/`triaged-out`, add a timing-tier marker (`end-gate-expanded` default vs `mid-flight`), and require a non-empty rationale whenever a finding is dismissed. The report-model section had to add three things: dismissed findings get rendered in the report (D3), an anti-rubber-stamp forcing function so the human can't trivially click approve (D4), and decision-grade presentation caps with a self-sufficiency floor (D5). Finally, a reconciliation note had to map each change back to both DEC-035 binding decision #1 and DEC-036. The contract's explicit fail conditions were: any residual unqualified "zero intermediate gates" or "ALWAYS auto-fixed" absolute, any missing required element, any widening of the mid-flight bar, or a missing/incomplete reconciliation note.

How it was checked. A QA agent read the revised spec against the eighteen required claims, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for places where the old absolutism survived. Be clear-eyed about what "verified" means here: the deliverable is a markdown design spec, and for this item verification was structured inspection of the produced text against the frozen contract — not execution. There is no runtime harness step for a document (the harness profile is `document-review`); nobody ran code, because there is no code in this item. The strength of the check is that every claim is a concrete, readable assertion and the reviewer confirmed each one literally appears in the file — which I independently re-confirmed: the mid-flight bar appears as exactly the two named conditions across §1, §8, and the report section with no third condition; the `escalated` disposition, the `end-gate-expanded | mid-flight` timing marker, the four stakes classes, the required dismissal rationale, the D3/D4/D5 report requirements, and the §14 reconciliation table all exist. The limit of the check is that it confirms the *spec reads correctly*, not that any engine built from it behaves correctly — that falls to the downstream build stories.

Result. PASS. The QA verdict recorded "18/18 required claims verified vs frozen .review.md; no fail criteria," with freeze status MATCH (the contract was not altered during the build). The adversarial review surfaced 8 findings, all judged legitimate and all fixed — notably several *residual* absolutes the first pass missed: cross-section statements in §2/§3 that still read as unqualified were re-qualified to acknowledge the mid-flight exception and the `escalated` outcome (F1–F3), mid-flight plumbing pointers were added to §3/§8 (F4), the `stakes_class` enum values were hyphenated for consistency (F5), a malformed table pipe in the §14 reconciliation table was repaired (F6), and two further residual code-fixer absolutes in §6/§10 were relaxed (R1/R2). Zero findings were escalated and zero dismissed. All three gates passed (dev: pass, qa: pass, code_review: pass-after-fix), and the item merged. The "diverged: true" flag reflects those in-build fixes, not a contract change — the frozen contract held.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. This story is a pre-build gate, not a feature. The conduct engine is being built by a sprint of downstream stories, and every one of those stories reads its design from `sprint-dev-redesign-spec.md`. The problem: that spec still encoded the *pre-amendment* absolutism — §8 said "zero intermediate gates" and §4 said legitimate findings are "ALWAYS auto-fixed" — which directly contradicts the decision the sprint exists to realize. No story in the original 52-item breakdown owned this revision, because each per-leg breakdown agent only saw its own slice of the spec, so none flagged the spec-as-a-unit-of-work. Left unrevised, every build agent would have faithfully re-encoded the exact absolutism the governing decision relaxed. So the chosen approach is to revise the *source of truth first* — fix the spec the engine stories build against — rather than patch each engine story after the fact. That is why this item touches only one markdown file and changes no executable code: it is correcting the design's premises so the build can't silently drift back to the old behavior.

Why it's built this way — the governing decisions. Two decisions drive this, per the story's References and Dev Notes. DEC-035 adopted conduct with a deliberately absolute anti-firehose stance: one human gate at the very end, zero intermediate approval gates, and legitimate findings always auto-fixed with no per-finding prompt — the goal being to end "specification fatigue" and stop the developer from being interrupted constantly. DEC-036 then *amends DEC-035 binding decision #1* narrowly: it keeps the anti-firehose intent but relaxes the absolutism in five specific ways (D1–D5) — (D1) a narrow, high-bar, stakes-gated mid-flight escalation tier bounded to irreversible-and-imminent OR build-invalidating; (D2) a stakes finding-classification with an `escalated` disposition so dangerous findings are raised rather than silently patched; (D3) the report must render what it dismissed, not only what it changed; (D4) an anti-rubber-stamp forcing function at the end-gate; and (D5) decision-grade presentation caps with a self-sufficiency floor. The revision targets exactly the four spec sections those decisions touch — §1 (binding decisions), §4 (fixer schema), §8 (gate model), and §9 (report model) — and adds §14, a reconciliation note that mirrors the DEC-036 Reconciliation table by mapping each spec change to DEC-035 binding decision #1 and to the specific DEC-036 sub-decision (D1–D5) it satisfies. The hard constraint baked into every edit, straight from DEC-036, is that the relaxation is *only of the absolutism, not the intent*: routine findings stay always auto-fixed, and the mid-flight tier is never widened beyond its two named conditions.

The file to look at in the diff below. This item changed a single file: `_bmad-output/planning-artifacts/sprint-dev-redesign-spec.md`. The substantive edits sit in §1 (binding decisions reworded to "end-gate default + narrow mid-flight exception"), §4 (the normalized finding schema now carries `stakes_class` and `timing_tier`; new Stakes-classes and Timing-tiers tables; the Disposition table gains `escalated` and enforces a non-empty `dismissed` rationale), §8 (the mid-flight escalation tier documented alongside the single end-gate), §9 (the D3 dismissed-rendering section, the D4 anti-rubber-stamp forcing function, and the D5 caps + self-sufficiency floor), and the new §14 reconciliation table — plus the in-build fix-pass touches to §2, §3, §6, and §10 that scrubbed the residual absolutes the adversarial reviewer caught.

The safety vocabulary — how problems are classified & resolved

The shared finding rulebookdirected-fix-finding-schema  had a high-risk divergence → §03

This story wrote the single shared rulebook that describes what a "review finding" looks like — the common format every reviewer (and the auto-fixer) must use to report a problem, so that nothing one reviewer flags gets lost or misread by the next step. Without this one agreed-upon shape, each reviewer would speak its own dialect and the fixer could silently fix the wrong things, drop findings, or have no consistent way to tell a routine problem apart from a serious one that needs a human's eyes.

Delivered: There is now a single canonical finding-schema document that every reviewer and the fixer read, spelling out each field, how serious problems get raised instead of silently fixed, and exactly how each finding must be dispositioned (fixed, dismissed, triaged to the backlog, or escalated).
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. This item is the one shared "finding" vocabulary that every reviewer (code-review, AVFL, qa-reviewer) and the auto-fixer in the conduct loop must speak. Its frozen contract required the produced document to nail down, in plain English a reader could verify without seeing any code, fifteen specific things: all twelve base fields a finding carries (story_slug, source, verdict, severity, type, location, summary, detail, evidence, ac_id, legitimate, suggested_fix), each with a meaning; a new `stakes_class` field that is explicitly *orthogonal to severity* (a finding has both, independently), defaults to `routine`, and lists exactly four values — `security-auth-isolation`, `irreversible-destructive` (with the migration / delete / force-push / prod-deploy examples), `high-blast-radius-architecture`, and `routine`; a `disposition` field whose values are exactly `fixed`, `dismissed`, `triaged-out`, `escalated`, with `escalated` defined as "raised, not silently fixed" and an explicit statement that there is no `deferred` disposition; a `timing_tier` marker of exactly `end-gate-expanded` (the default) and `mid-flight`, where mid-flight is constrained to a deliberately narrow bar (irreversible-and-imminent OR build-invalidating only) that must not be widened, with end-gate-expanded named as the safety net; a hard rule that a `dismissed` finding must carry a non-empty rationale (an empty wave-off is invalid); and the relaxed auto-fix invariant spelled out both ways — a *routine* legitimate finding is always auto-fixed, while a *stakes-class* legitimate finding is escalated rather than silently fixed, with the routine fall-through stated explicitly so the common case stays cheap.

How it was checked. Because this is a schema/spec document (a `config-structure` change), its declared verification method is document-review, not execution. A QA agent read the produced `finding-schema.md` against the frozen review contract and confirmed each required claim line by line; an independent adversarial reviewer then hunted the document for gaps, contradictions, and missing edge cases. Be clear about the strength of this: there is no code that runs here and the runtime test harness for this sprint is in "skip" mode, so "verified" means the document was inspected against a frozen, itemized contract — not that anything was executed. What that buys you is high confidence the document *says* everything DEC-036 requires it to say; it does not and cannot prove downstream consumers actually obey it (that is the job of the stories that build the fixer, escalation, and end-gate on top of this schema).

Result. The verdict is PASS, recorded as freeze MATCH (the build was checked against the same contract that was frozen before work began) with QA evidence of "15/15 required claims verified vs frozen .review.md." Two legitimate findings surfaced during review and both were fixed before merge: the adversarial reviewer noted that `triaged-out` lacked a reachability path for legitimate-but-out-of-scope findings, so a routing rule was added; and that `severity`/`source`/`verdict` could be misread as closed enumerations, so they were marked as non-exhaustive examples that point back to spec section 4 as the source of truth. Zero findings were escalated or dismissed. Gates landed dev: pass, qa: pass, code_review: pass-after-fix, and the item merged. The honest caveat stands: this is inspection-quality assurance of a specification document, the strongest verification available for an artifact that has no executable behavior.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. The conduct build loop has four parties that all touch "findings" — three reviewers (code-review, AVFL, qa-reviewer) and one fixer — plus the report and the human end-gate that render them. Rather than let each reviewer emit its own ad-hoc format (the legacy sprint-dev failure mode, where the auto-fix loop was an opaque firehose that silently fixed or silently dropped things), this item makes the finding shape a single, referenceable document that everyone normalizes into and reads from. It is deliberately the *hard root* of the whole escalation chain: it lands first, and every downstream leg (fixer stakes-routing, mid-flight escalation, disposition rendering in the report, the anti-rubber-stamp end-gate) blocks on it. The vocabulary change it carries is small in surface area but absolute in effect — if the disposition values and the stakes-class axis are not nailed down here, nothing downstream can be built consistently, so the design front-loads that keystone into one schema.

Why this exact shape — the governing decisions. The schema is the direct deliverable for conduct spec section 4 (the canonical normalized finding schema), cited in this item's Dev Notes, and it encodes two stacked decisions. DEC-035 ruled that the auto-fix loop must be *legible*: it must show both what it changed and what it dismissed, organize the report by user-facing functionality, and end at one human gate. That is why the schema carries a `disposition` vocabulary at all and why a `dismissed` finding must carry a non-empty rationale — a legible record can never be an empty wave-off. DEC-036 then amended DEC-035's first binding decision narrowly: not every legitimate finding may be silently auto-fixed. Stakes-class findings — the ones with real consequences — must leave the silent auto-fix path and be *raised*. That single amendment is the source of the four new schema elements: the `stakes_class` axis orthogonal to severity (with `routine` default), the new `escalated` disposition meaning "raised, not silently fixed" (and the explicit exclusion of `deferred`, so findings are never parked), the `timing_tier` marker with a deliberately *narrow, non-widenable* mid-flight bar and end-gate-expanded as the default safety net, and the relaxed invariant that routine legitimate findings stay always-auto-fixed (preserving DEC-036's anti-firehose intent) while only stakes-class legitimate findings escalate. The narrow-bar discipline and the routine fall-through are hard requirements from DEC-036, not stylistic preferences — the schema is written so no downstream consumer can quietly widen the escalation path.

What changed in the diff below. A single new file: `skills/momentum/references/finding-schema.md` (the canonical schema document, ~174 lines). Nothing executable changed — this item produces the spec that later conduct stories implement against. The two review fixes are also visible in this file: a `triaged-out` routing rule for legitimate-but-out-of-scope findings, and a note that `severity`/`source`/`verdict` are illustrative rather than closed enumerations, deferring to spec section 4.

▶ ③ The actual diff  (1 file changed, 174 insertions(+))
diff --git a/skills/momentum/references/finding-schema.md b/skills/momentum/references/finding-schema.mdnew file mode 100644index 0000000..c2d9547--- /dev/null+++ b/skills/momentum/references/finding-schema.md@@ -0,0 +1,174 @@+# Canonical Normalized Finding Schema++**Version:** 1.0 — established with DEC-035 + DEC-036 (stakes-class escalation amendment)++This is the single shared finding schema that every reviewer (code-review, AVFL, qa-reviewer) and the fixer speak. Reviewers normalize their raw output into this shape before the fixer sees it. The fixer reads only this shape. The report and the end-gate render only this shape. No finding travels the conduct directed-fix chain in any other format.++---++## Base Fields++Every finding carries all of the following fields.++| Field | Type | Meaning |+|---|---|---|+| `story_slug` | string | The slug of the story the finding is against. Ties the finding to a specific unit of work. |+| `source` | string | Which reviewer produced the finding — e.g. `bmad-code-review`, `avfl`, `qa-reviewer`, `architecture-guard`, `e2e-validator`, `simplify`. Open string; listed values are non-exhaustive examples. |+| `verdict` | string | The reviewer's raw verdict on this issue — e.g. `PASS`, `FAIL`, `BLOCKED`. Distinct from `disposition` (disposition is what happens to the finding; verdict is what the reviewer assessed). Open string; listed values are non-exhaustive examples. |+| `severity` | string | The reviewer's severity assessment — e.g. `blocker`, `critical`, `major`, `minor`, `low`. Orthogonal to `stakes_class` — see below. Open string; listed values are non-exhaustive examples. |+| `type` | string | Category of issue — e.g. `correctness`, `security`, `spec-compliance`, `style`, `test-coverage`. |+| `location` | string | Where in the artifact the issue appears — file path, line range, function name, or step reference. |+| `summary` | string | One-sentence plain-English description of the problem. Used in collapsed views and report headers. |+| `detail` | string | Full explanation: what is wrong, why it matters, and what the expected state is. |+| `evidence` | string | The concrete artifact excerpt (code snippet, spec quote, test output) that substantiates the finding. |+| `ac_id` | string or null | Acceptance criterion identifier this finding maps to, if any. Null when the finding is not tied to a specific AC. |+| `legitimate` | boolean | Whether the reviewer judges the finding to be a genuine issue (true) or a false positive (false). Drives the auto-fix invariant — see Disposition Rules below. |+| `suggested_fix` | string or null | The reviewer's proposed remediation, if one is available. The fixer uses this as a starting point; null when the reviewer offers none. |++---++## Stakes Class++```+stakes_class: security-auth-isolation | irreversible-destructive | high-blast-radius-architecture | routine+```++**Default:** `routine`++`stakes_class` is **orthogonal to `severity`**. A finding has both independently. A finding may be low-severity yet stakes-class (e.g., a small insecure default in an auth path), or high-severity yet routine (e.g., a major logic bug in a UI component with no blast radius beyond that component). Severity measures the magnitude of the problem in isolation; stakes class measures the consequences of mis-handling it.++Reviewers assign `stakes_class` alongside `severity`. When a finding does not meet any of the three stakes criteria below, `stakes_class` is `routine`.++### Defined Stakes Classes++**`security-auth-isolation`**+Security, authentication, or isolation findings. Covers XSS, injection, privilege escalation, auth bypass, leaked credentials, inadequate sandboxing, and any finding where a mistake opens an exploit path or breaks a trust boundary.++**`irreversible-destructive`**+Actions that cannot be undone or that destroy data/state with no safe rollback. Concrete examples: schema migrations, data deletes, force-pushes, production deploys, destructive file operations, and any change whose failure mode involves permanent loss.++**`high-blast-radius-architecture`**+Changes with wide downstream effect — architectural decisions, cross-cutting concerns, shared interfaces, or design patterns that, if wrong, cascade across many consumers or require large-scale rework to correct.++**`routine`**+Any finding that does not meet the criteria of the three stakes classes above. This is the common case. Routine findings follow the always-auto-fixed path (see Disposition Rules).++---++## Disposition++```+disposition: fixed | dismissed | triaged-out | escalated+```++The disposition records what happened to the finding after the fixer processed it.++| Value | Meaning |+|---|---|+| `fixed` | The fixer applied a fix. The finding is resolved. |+| `dismissed` | The fixer judged the finding not worth acting on. **Requires a non-empty rationale** — see Required-Rationale Rule below. |+| `triaged-out` | The finding is valid but out of scope for this story (e.g., belongs to a different component, tracked separately). Triaged-out findings appear in the report's out-of-scope section; they are not silently dropped. |+| `escalated` | The finding was raised for human attention rather than quietly resolved by the auto-fix path. This is the disposition for stakes-class legitimate findings. The finding travels to the end-gate (or, under the narrow mid-flight bar, to an immediate escalation point) as a decision card. |++### No Deferred Disposition++`deferred` is not a valid disposition value. Findings are never parked in a deferred state. Every finding that enters the fixer receives one of the four dispositions above before the fix loop exits. A finding that cannot be fixed, dismissed, or triaged-out is escalated.++---++## Required-Rationale Rule++When `disposition` is `dismissed`, the finding **must** carry a non-empty `dismissal_rationale` string. A dismissal with a missing or empty rationale is invalid by schema and must be treated as a schema violation. The legible auto-fix record (per DEC-035 D5) is only as good as its dismissal explanations — empty wave-offs defeat its purpose.++```+dismissal_rationale: string   # required when disposition == "dismissed"; non-empty+```++---++## Disposition Rules — The Relaxed Auto-Fix Invariant++The fixer applies the following rules when processing a finding. These rules encode the DEC-036 amendment to DEC-035's binding decision #1.++**Rule 1 — Routine legitimate findings are always auto-fixed.**++When `legitimate` is `true` and `stakes_class` is `routine`, the fixer applies a fix and sets `disposition` to `fixed`. No human prompt; no escalation. This is the common-case path and preserves the anti-firehose intent: the vast majority of findings are handled autonomously without interrupting the human.++**Rule 2 — Stakes-class legitimate findings are escalated, not silently auto-fixed.**++When `legitimate` is `true` and `stakes_class` is one of `security-auth-isolation`, `irreversible-destructive`, or `high-blast-radius-architecture`, the fixer sets `disposition` to `escalated`. The finding is raised for human attention rather than quietly resolved. The timing tier (see below) determines whether escalation is immediate (mid-flight) or deferred to the end-gate.++This rule is the relaxation of DEC-035's absolutism ("legitimate issues are *always* fixed automatically"). The relaxation is narrow: only stakes-class findings leave the silent auto-fix path. Routine findings remain always auto-fixed.++**Rule 3 — Non-legitimate findings are dismissed.**++When `legitimate` is `false`, the fixer sets `disposition` to `dismissed` with a non-empty rationale. Non-legitimate findings are never fixed, triaged-out, or escalated.++**Rule 4 — Legitimate but out-of-scope findings are triaged-out.**++When `legitimate` is `true` but the finding is out of scope for this story (e.g., it belongs to a different component or requires work tracked separately), the fixer sets `disposition` to `triaged-out` and spins a backlog stub via momentum:triage. Triaged-out findings appear in the report's out-of-scope section; they are not silently dropped.++### Severity and Stakes Class Are Independent Axes++Severity and stakes class are assessed independently. The disposition rules reference `stakes_class`, not `severity`, as the escalation trigger. A critical-severity routine finding is still auto-fixed (Rule 1). A minor-severity security finding is still escalated (Rule 2). Both axes appear in the report and in the end-gate decision cards so the human has full context.++---++## Timing Tier++```+timing_tier: end-gate-expanded | mid-flight+```++**Default:** `end-gate-expanded`++The timing tier applies to `escalated` findings. It marks when the escalation is surfaced to the human.++| Value | Meaning |+|---|---|+| `end-gate-expanded` | The finding is held until the end-gate, where it appears as a decision card alongside the full conduct report. This is the default tier and the safety net — all escalated findings belong here unless they meet the narrow mid-flight bar. |+| `mid-flight` | The finding triggers an immediate escalation before the fix loop continues. This is reserved for findings that meet the high bar below. |++### The Narrow Mid-Flight Bar++The `mid-flight` tier applies **only** to escalated findings that meet **at least one** of the following conditions:++1. **Irreversible-and-imminent** — the finding describes an action that is both irreversible (cannot be undone) and about to be executed as part of the current build step. Continuing would make recovery impossible.+2. **Build-invalidating** — the finding reveals that the build as currently structured cannot produce a valid artifact and continuing would compound the invalid state (e.g., a foundational spec contradiction that makes all downstream stories invalid).++This bar is **intentionally narrow** and **must not be widened**. Urgency alone is not sufficient. Stakes class alone is not sufficient. The combination of irreversibility-or-build-invalidity with imminence is what triggers mid-flight. All other escalated findings — including all security, architecture, and destructive-action findings that do not meet this bar — belong to the default `end-gate-expanded` tier.++The end-gate-expanded tier is the safety net. When in doubt, the finding belongs there.++---++## Full Finding Shape (Reference)++```yaml+# Required base fields+story_slug: string+source: string                        # bmad-code-review | avfl | qa-reviewer | architecture-guard | e2e-validator | simplify | ... (open string)+verdict: string                       # PASS | FAIL | BLOCKED | ... (open string; reviewer's raw verdict)+severity: string                      # blocker | critical | major | minor | low | ... (open string)+stakes_class: security-auth-isolation | irreversible-destructive | high-blast-radius-architecture | routine+type: string                          # correctness | security | spec-compliance | style | test-coverage | ...+location: string+summary: string                       # one sentence+detail: string                        # full explanation+evidence: string                      # artifact excerpt+ac_id: string | null+legitimate: boolean+suggested_fix: string | null++# Fixer-assigned fields (set during fix loop, not by reviewer)+disposition: fixed | dismissed | triaged-out | escalated+dismissal_rationale: string           # required and non-empty when disposition == "dismissed"+timing_tier: end-gate-expanded | mid-flight   # set when disposition == "escalated"; defaults to end-gate-expanded+```++---++## Source Decisions++- **DEC-035** — Adopt conduct; one human end-gate; no story-count cap; report organized by user-facing functionality; legible auto-fix loop showing what it changed AND what it dismissed.+- **DEC-036** — Amends DEC-035 binding decision #1 narrowly: stakes-and-timing escalation policy; stakes-class findings leave the silent auto-fix path; report renders dismissals; anti-rubber-stamp end-gate; routine findings stay always auto-fixed; anti-firehose intent preserved. The mid-flight bar stays narrow by design.
The risk-classification rulebookstakes-classification-rubric

This story wrote the single rulebook conduct uses to decide how serious any problem found during a build is — sorting every finding into one of three high-stakes buckets (security and access concerns; things that can't be safely undone like deletes, migrations, or production deploys; and changes whose failure spreads widely) or else "routine," and deciding the rare cases that must interrupt the build immediately versus the ones that wait for the final human review. Without one shared rulebook, the three different parts of the build pipeline that flag problems would each judge severity their own way, so a genuinely dangerous finding could be downgraded to "routine" and silently auto-fixed-and-shipped by one part while another would have escalated it — the exact inconsistency that lets serious risks slip past the human gate.

Delivered: There is now one authoritative, referenceable rubric document that defines every stakes class, the routine fall-through, and the deliberately narrow rule for when a finding interrupts the build mid-flight versus waiting for the expanded end-gate — written so the code-review adapter, the qa-reviewer, and AVFL all classify against it instead of each inventing their own classifier.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. The frozen contract for this item was a document-review checklist, not an executable test — the deliverable is a written rubric, so "verification" means a fresh reader can confirm every required claim by reading that one file alone. Concretely, the rubric had to: name exactly three stakes classes (security/auth-isolation, irreversible/destructive, high-blast-radius/architecture) plus a `routine` fall-through, and assert those four are exhaustive and mutually exclusive so every finding lands in exactly one; give each of the three stakes classes a list of concrete, recognizable signals a producer can match against (with migrations, deletes, force-pushes, and production deploys explicitly named for the destructive class); state that any finding matching none of the three is `routine` and stays on the silent auto-fix path, never escalated; name the two timing tiers (`end-gate-expanded` as default, `mid-flight` as narrow); state the mid-flight bar as irreversible-and-imminent OR build-invalidating ONLY, with everything else — including stakes-class findings that fail that test — defaulting to `end-gate-expanded`; instruct the reader to bias narrow when in doubt, declare the mid-flight bar must never widen, and name `end-gate-expanded` as the safety net; claim sole ownership of classification logic and tell the three producers (code-review adapter, qa-reviewer, AVFL) to reference it rather than fork their own; and express its output in vocabulary that drops straight into the finding schema's `stakes_class` and timing-tier fields without translation. The contract's fail criteria were the mirror image — most pointedly, a mid-flight tier described any broader than the narrow bar (e.g., "any stakes-class finding escalates mid-flight"), a missing never-widen guardrail, or wording that lets producers define their own classifier.

How it was checked. A QA agent verified the built rubric against those required claims, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for contradictions and ambiguity; the workflow re-checks high-risk items after fixes. Be clear about how strong that is: this is a markdown specification file, and the runtime verification harness for this sprint is in "skip" mode, so nothing was executed. Verification here was structured inspection of the document against the frozen contract — reading the rubric and confirming each claim is present and unambiguous — not a live run. "Verified" here means "inspected and matched against the contract," not "exercised by a test."

Result. The verdict was PASS with the freeze recorded as MATCH (the contract was not altered mid-build). Two findings were raised during review and both were fixed (recorded as "internal-contradiction + clarity fixes"); zero were escalated and zero dismissed. One notable evidence point: the reviewer caught the build making an out-of-scope edit to the conductor's `workflow.md`, and the Conductor reverted it — the rubric-vs-conductor bar wording was deliberately left for the separate escalation-mechanism story to reconcile, so this item stayed inside its boundary. The merged deliverable is the single file `skills/momentum/references/stakes-classification-rubric.md`. The honest summary: the document fully satisfies the frozen checklist on inspection, and its correctness rests on that inspection rather than on execution.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. Three legs of the conduct build pipeline — the code-review adapter, the qa-reviewer, and AVFL — each independently needed to tag findings with a stakes class, and each had proposed a near-identical classifier of its own. Three divergent classifiers would drift apart over time and produce inconsistent escalation behavior. The choice made here is single-source: author the classification logic once, as a human-readable reference document, and have every producer read from it via small emission-wiring rather than re-deriving the rules. The rubric itself states this discipline explicitly — it claims sole ownership of the logic and declares that forking it into a producer is a violation. That is why this is one story producing one file, with the producers receiving only thin wiring in their own stories. The companion `directed-fix-finding-schema` story supplied the structural half (the `stakes_class` field and the disposition/timing vocabulary); this story supplies the semantic half (which value goes in that field), and the rubric's output vocabulary is kept identical to the schema's so a producer can copy a verdict straight across.

Why it's built this way — governing decisions. The shape is driven directly by two binding decisions and an assessment finding cited in the story's References and Dev Notes. DEC-035 establishes conduct's always-on, silent auto-fix path with a single human end-gate and no story-count cap — which is why `routine` is the common case and routine findings are always auto-fixed and never escalated. DEC-036 narrowly amends DEC-035's binding decision #1 to permit a high-bar, stakes-gated mid-flight escalation tier: stakes-class findings can leave the silent path, dismissals are rendered with rationale, and the end-gate is anti-rubber-stamp — while the anti-firehose intent is preserved (only the absolutism was relaxed). This is the source of the entire timing-tier design, including the deliberately narrow mid-flight bar (irreversible-and-imminent OR build-invalidating ONLY), the bias-narrow instruction, the never-widen guardrail, and `end-gate-expanded` named as the safety net. The Dev Notes map these to the conduct spec sections: §1 (DEC-035) for the silent path and single end-gate, and §2 (DEC-036) plus the DEC-036 mid-flight bar definition for the escalation tier and its guardrails. The work also closes AES-004 Finding 2, which graded the stakes heuristic as "missing/unwired — no heuristic flags high-risk"; this rubric *is* that heuristic, authored once. All three sources are restated in the rubric's own "Source Decisions" section, so the document is self-grounding.

What changed in the diff. This story added exactly one file: `skills/momentum/references/stakes-classification-rubric.md`. The diff below is the full rubric — purpose statement, the three stakes-class definitions with their signal lists, the routine fall-through, the timing-tier table and narrow mid-flight bar, the never-widen guardrail, the per-producer consumption notes, and the source-decision citations. No other files are part of this item (the out-of-scope `conductor/workflow.md` edit caught during review was reverted).

Do not assign routine affirmatively — it is the absence of a stakes-class match, not a positive characteristic of the finding itself.++---++## Timing-Tier Decision Rule++The timing tier applies only to **escalated** findings (`disposition: escalated`). Continuing would make recovery impossible.+2. **Build-invalidating** — the finding reveals that the build as currently structured cannot produce a valid artifact and that continuing would compound the invalid state (e.g., a foundational spec contradiction that makes all downstream stories invalid).++**Stakes class alone is not sufficient to trigger `mid-flight`.** Urgency alone is not sufficient. Irreversibility paired with imminence, OR build-invalidity on its own, is what crosses the bar. A finding held to the end-gate is not lost; it is reviewed with full context. It must not be extended to cover:++- All stakes-class findings (only irreversible-and-imminent or build-invalidating ones)+- Urgency or business priority alone+- Reviewer judgment that "this one is really important"+- Any expansion decided unilaterally by a producer++Every proposed widening must be treated as a spec change, not a producer-level decision. The fixer hand-off contractdirected-fix-invocation-contract

This story wrote the rulebook for how conduct's automatic fixer hands its results back to the orchestrator that runs the build: for every problem review finds, the fixer must report exactly what it did with it — fixed it, dismissed it with a reason, set it aside, or flagged it as too dangerous to silently fix and raised it for a human instead. Without this contract, a high-stakes problem (a database migration, a delete, a force-push, a security gap) could get quietly auto-fixed and buried alongside trivial ones, with no reliable way for the orchestrator to tell which findings were handled and which need human eyes.

Delivered: There is now a written contract of record defining the four ways a finding can be resolved, the rule that dangerous stakes-class findings are escalated to a human with the full what/why/evidence carried inline rather than auto-fixed, and the boundary that the fixer reports its dispositions but never pauses or prompts on its own.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. This item is a contract spec — a reference document describing the agreement between the Conductor (the top-level orchestrator that owns all git changes, routing, and the single human end-gate) and the directed `momentum:dev` fix-mode (the subagent that actually applies fixes during the autonomous build). The frozen contract did not test code; it listed twelve plain-language claims the document had to make, and it would fail if any were absent, contradicted, or stated so vaguely a reviewer couldn't confirm them. In plain terms, the document had to say: (1) it's the invocation contract for the directed fix-mode used during the build phase; (2) the shape is "findings go in, applied fixes plus a per-finding disposition come out," with exactly one disposition per finding and none dropped; (3) the only legal dispositions are `fixed`, `dismissed`, `triaged-out`, and `escalated`, each with a one-line meaning; (4) the routine path is unchanged and always-on — an ordinary finding gets quietly fixed and committed; (5) the new escalate-do-not-fix path — a high-stakes finding comes back as `escalated` with an inline explanation instead of a fix, and explicitly produces no fix and no fix commit; (6) the three stakes classes that trigger escalation (security/auth-isolation; irreversible/destructive like migrations, deletes, force-pushes, prod deploys; high-blast-radius/architecture), with `routine` as the default; (7) the escalation payload must be carried inline and contain at minimum the what/why/evidence a human needs to decide — not a pointer to fetch later; (8) a timing-tier flag with only two values, `end-gate-expanded` (the default) and `mid-flight` (a narrow exception restricted to irreversible-and-imminent OR build-invalidating findings only); (9) any `dismissed` finding must carry a non-empty rationale; (10) an explicit statement that this contract does NOT own or implement the pause — the fix-mode emits a disposition and timing flag and never pauses, blocks, or prompts the human itself.

How it was checked. A QA agent read the produced document against the frozen `.review.md` claim list, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for gaps, contradictions, and the specific failure modes the contract called out (conflating escalation with fixing, a too-broad mid-flight bar, a payload described as a pointer, a dismissal without rationale). High-risk findings were re-checked after fixes. Be honest about the strength of this: this is a markdown specification document and the runtime harness profile is document-review, so verification was structured inspection of the written prose against the frozen contract — not live execution. Nothing was run; "verified" here means a reviewer confirmed each required claim is present and unambiguous in the text, not that any code exercised the behavior. That's appropriate for a contract spec (there is no executable behavior to run), but it does mean the assurance is "the document says the right things," not "the system does the right things."

The build record shows this reached PASS on round 3 with the freeze recorded as MATCH (the deliverable matched the frozen contract), three findings raised during review, all three fixed, zero escalated, zero dismissed, and the post-fix recheck CLEAN. No fix commit is produced."), the inline what/why/evidence floor, the two-value timing tier with an explicitly narrow mid-flight bar, the non-empty dismissal-rationale rule, and the pause-ownership disclaimer are all stated unambiguously rather than implied.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. The conduct rewrite of `momentum:sprint-dev` runs the whole build autonomously and only stops for the human once, at the end. For that to be safe, the Conductor needs a clean, machine-readable answer back for every review finding the fix-mode handles — what was fixed, what was waved off and why, and crucially which findings were too high-stakes to fix silently. This document specifies exactly that handoff as a single narrow shape: findings in, applied fixes plus a per-finding disposition out. The key design move is the new `escalated` disposition and its escalate-do-not-fix path: when a finding touches security, an irreversible/destructive action, or wide-blast-radius architecture, the fix-mode deliberately does not fix it and does not commit anything — it returns the finding as an inline decision card and lets the Conductor decide whether and when to surface it. The other deliberate move is the boundary: the fix-mode emits a disposition and a timing flag and stops; it never pauses or prompts. That separation is what keeps the autonomous loop from either turning into a finding firehose or springing surprise pauses — the fix-mode classifies, the Conductor routes.

Why it's built this way — governing decisions. Per the story's References and Dev Notes, two decisions drive this. DEC-035 adopts conduct: one human end-gate, no story-count cap, and a legible auto-fix loop that reports both what it changed and what it dismissed — which is why the routine path stays always-on and silent, and why dismissals must carry rationale and be renderable in the report. DEC-036 amends DEC-035's binding decision #1 narrowly, and four of its amendments are folded directly into this contract: D1 (the disposition vocabulary including `escalated`, and the stakes-and-timing escalation policy), D2 (the stakes-class escalate-do-not-fix path that returns an escalation payload instead of a fix commit), D3 (non-empty rationale required on `dismissed`, plus rendering dismissals in the report), and D5 (the inline-payload floor — the self-sufficient what/why/evidence decision card). The contract is careful to honor DEC-035's anti-firehose intent (routine findings never interrupt the human) while permitting DEC-036's high-bar relaxation (stakes-class findings get raised, not buried), and it explicitly keeps the mid-flight bar narrow so the exception can't quietly widen back into a firehose.

What changed in the diff. This item added one new file: `skills/momentum/references/directed-fix-invocation-contract.md`. It is a `specification`-type artifact that introduces no executable behavior — it documents the contract and references its sibling `skills/momentum/references/finding-schema.md` (the inbound finding shape, including `stakes_class` and `legitimate`) rather than re-deriving that vocabulary. The diff below is the document itself; reading the Purpose, Escalate-Do-Not-Fix Path, Inline Escalation Payload, Timing Tier, and Pause-Ownership Boundary sections is the fastest way to confirm the design.

The Conductor is the top-level session orchestrator that owns all git mutation, routing, and the single human end-gate. The directed fix-mode is a subagent invoked by the Conductor; it applies fixes and returns per-finding dispositions, but it never spawns additional humans-in-the-loop and never owns the pause/routing decision. This contract defines the seam between those two roles precisely enough that neither role bleeds into the other.++---++## Contract Shape++The contract is a single, narrow invocation shape:++> **Findings in → {applied fixes, per-finding dispositions} out.**++The fix-mode accepts a set of inbound findings — each conforming to the Canonical Normalized Finding Schema (see `finding-schema.md`) — and returns, for every finding, exactly one disposition plus, where applicable, the change it applied. No finding leaves the fix-mode without a disposition. There is no partial result, no deferred finding, and no silent drop.++---++## Disposition Vocabulary++Every finding processed by the fix-mode exits with exactly one of the following disposition values. These values are authoritative; no other value is valid.++| Disposition | Meaning |+|---|---|+| `fixed` | A fix was applied to the finding. The finding is resolved. |+| `dismissed` | The finding was judged not a genuine issue (false positive). **Requires a non-empty rationale** — see Dismissed-Rationale Rule below. |+| `triaged-out` | The finding is legitimate but out of scope for this story; it is tracked separately via a `momentum:triage` backlog stub and rendered in the report's out-of-scope section (per `finding-schema.md` Rule 4) — it is not silently dropped. |+| `escalated` | The finding was raised for human attention rather than quietly resolved by the auto-fix path. This disposition is returned **instead of** applying a fix or producing a fix commit. |++### No Deferred Disposition++`deferred` is not a valid disposition value. Every inbound finding receives one of the four values above before the fix-mode exits. A finding that cannot be fixed, dismissed, or triaged-out must be escalated.++---++## Routine Path (Unchanged, Always-On Default)++When an inbound finding is `legitimate: true` and `stakes_class: routine`, the fix-mode applies a fix and returns `disposition: fixed` with the change committed. This path is **unchanged from prior behavior** and is the always-on default — the vast majority of findings follow this path autonomously without any interruption or escalation. This preserves the anti-firehose intent of DEC-035: routine findings never reach the human before the end-gate.++---++## Escalate-Do-Not-Fix Path (DEC-036 Amendment D2)++When an inbound finding is `legitimate: true` and `stakes_class` is one of the three stakes classes below, the fix-mode returns `disposition: escalated` together with an inline escalation payload. **No fix is applied. No fix commit is produced.** The finding travels to the Conductor as a decision card; only the Conductor decides whether and when to surface it to the human.++This path is the narrow relaxation of DEC-035's original absolutism. It applies only to stakes-class findings; routine findings remain always auto-fixed.++---++## Stakes Classes++The `stakes_class` field on an inbound finding (defined in `finding-schema.md`) determines which path is taken.++**Default class:** `routine` — any finding that does not meet the criteria of the three stakes classes below.++**`security-auth-isolation`**+Security, authentication, or isolation findings. Covers XSS, injection, privilege escalation, auth bypass, leaked credentials, inadequate sandboxing, and any finding where a mistake opens an exploit path or breaks a trust boundary. Triggers the escalate-do-not-fix path.++**`irreversible-destructive`**+Actions that cannot be undone or that destroy data/state with no safe rollback. Concrete examples: schema migrations, data deletes, force-pushes, production deploys, destructive file operations, and any change whose failure mode involves permanent loss. Triggers the escalate-do-not-fix path.++**`high-blast-radius-architecture`**+Changes with wide downstream effect — architectural decisions, cross-cutting concerns, shared interfaces, or design patterns that, if wrong, cascade across many consumers or require large-scale rework to correct. Triggers the escalate-do-not-fix path.++---++## Inline Escalation Payload (DEC-036 Amendment D5)++When a finding is escalated, the fix-mode's return MUST carry the escalation payload **inline** — embedded directly in the response, not as a pointer or external reference that the Conductor must fetch. The payload must contain, at minimum:++- **What** — a clear statement of the finding: what issue was detected and where.+- **Why** — the explanation of why this finding requires human attention rather than auto-fix: what stakes class applies and what the consequences of mis-handling are.+- **Evidence** — the concrete artifact excerpt (code snippet, spec quote, test output, or similar) that substantiates the finding and gives the human enough context to adjudicate without going back to the raw source.++The payload is a self-contained human decision card. It must be sufficient for a human to make an informed decision without additional retrieval.++---++## Timing Tier (DEC-036 Escalation Timing)++The escalation payload MUST also carry a `timing_tier` flag with one of exactly two values:++| Value | Meaning |+|---|---|+| `end-gate-expanded` | The escalation is held until the end-gate, where it appears as a decision card alongside the full conduct report. **This is the default.** |+| `mid-flight` | The escalation is flagged as requiring immediate attention before the fix loop continues. This is a narrow, high-bar exception. |++**Default:** `end-gate-expanded`. When in doubt, the finding belongs at the end-gate.++### The Narrow Mid-Flight Bar++`mid-flight` applies **only** to escalated findings that meet **at least one** of the following conditions:++1. **Irreversible-and-imminent** — the finding describes an action that is both irreversible (cannot be undone) and about to be executed as part of the current build step. Continuing would make recovery impossible.+2. **Build-invalidating** — the finding reveals that the build as currently structured cannot produce a valid artifact, and continuing would compound the invalid state (e.g., a foundational spec contradiction that makes all downstream stories invalid).++**This bar is intentionally narrow and must not be widened.** Urgency alone is not sufficient. Stakes class alone is not sufficient. Only the combination of irreversibility-or-build-invalidity with imminence triggers `mid-flight`. All other escalated findings — including all security, architecture, and destructive-action findings that do not meet this bar — belong to `end-gate-expanded`.++---++## Dismissed-Rationale Rule (DEC-035 D5 / DEC-036 D3)++When `disposition` is `dismissed`, the fix-mode's return MUST carry a non-empty `dismissal_rationale` string explaining why the finding was dismissed. A dismissal with a missing or empty rationale is invalid. Empty wave-offs defeat the legible auto-fix record required by DEC-035 D5 — dismissal explanations are not optional. DEC-036 D3 extends this legibility requirement by mandating that dismissals be rendered in the conduct report (the "Dismissed / not-actioned" section), but the underlying non-empty-rationale requirement is grounded in DEC-035 D5.++---++## Pause-Ownership Boundary++This contract defines what the fix-mode returns. It does **not** own or implement the mid-flight pause mechanism.++The fix-mode emits a `disposition` value and, for escalated findings, a `timing_tier` flag. That is the full extent of its routing output. The Conductor — which owns all git mutation, routing, and the single human end-gate — reads the disposition and timing-tier and decides whether and when to pause, prompt, or route the escalation. The fix-mode itself **never pauses, never blocks, and never prompts the human**. Consuming the timing-tier is the Conductor's responsibility, not the fix-mode's.++This boundary is stated explicitly to prevent the fix-mode from re-introducing a finding firehose or surprise pauses during the autonomous build phase.++---++## Disposition Rules Summary++These rules encode the DEC-036 amendment to DEC-035's binding decision #1.++| Condition | Disposition |+|---|---|+| `legitimate: true`, `stakes_class: routine` | `fixed` — auto-fixed and committed; no escalation |+| `legitimate: true`, `stakes_class` is `security-auth-isolation`, `irreversible-destructive`, or `high-blast-radius-architecture` | `escalated` — inline payload returned; no fix applied; no fix commit |+| `legitimate: false` | `dismissed` — non-empty rationale required; never fixed or escalated |+| `legitimate: true`, out of scope for this story | `triaged-out` — tracked separately; not silently dropped |++---++## Source Decisions++- **DEC-035** — Adopt conduct; one human end-gate; no story-count cap; report organized by user-facing functionality; legible auto-fix loop showing what it changed AND what it dismissed.+- **DEC-036** — Amends DEC-035 binding decision #1 narrowly: D1 = stakes-and-timing escalation policy (the centerpiece — defines the three stakes classes, two timing tiers, and the narrow mid-flight bar); D2 = add a stakes finding-class to the fixer schema and hold stakes-class findings out of silent auto-fix; D3 = render the `dismissed` disposition in the conduct report (builds the unbuilt half of DEC-035 D5's legibility requirement); D5 = establish a decision-grade presentation standard with a self-sufficiency floor (the inline `what / why / evidence` payload requirement); anti-firehose intent and always-auto-fix for routine findings are preserved.++---++## Related References++- `skills/momentum/references/finding-schema.md` — Canonical Normalized Finding Schema; defines the inbound finding shape (including `stakes_class`, `legitimate`, `suggested_fix`) and the disposition and timing-tier fields this contract assigns.

The fixer — acting on what review finds

The fixer's fix-or-raise decisiondev-fix-mode-entry  had a high-risk divergence → §03

This piece teaches the builder's worker how to act on a problem that review found: quietly fix and commit ordinary problems on its own, but for high-stakes problems (anything touching security, anything destructive or irreversible like a migration or a force-push, or anything that reshapes the architecture) make no change and instead raise the issue to a human for a decision. The guarantee it gives the conduct system is that dangerous fixes never get applied silently and only land in front of the human when they genuinely warrant it — without this dividing line, either a risky change gets auto-committed and buried, or every trivial nit interrupts the human and the single end-of-build review turns into a firehose.

Delivered: The builder's worker can now take a directed problem report, sort it by how risky the fix is, auto-fix and commit the safe ones, and hand the dangerous ones back to a human as a self-contained escalation that says what the problem is, why it's risky, the evidence, and how urgent it is.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. The frozen contract pinned six behaviors. First, handing the dev agent a *directed-fix payload* (a finding raised by the Conductor) must put it into a new "fix-mode" — distinct from its normal green-field build. Second, a routine finding that is legitimate must be silently fixed: the named file(s) get edited and a commit is produced, with no escalation. Third, a stakes-class finding (security/auth-isolation, an irreversible or destructive action like a migration/delete/force-push/prod-deploy, or a high-blast-radius architecture issue) that is legitimate must produce no edit and no commit — instead the agent returns an *escalation*. Fourth, that escalation has to be self-contained: read on its own with no outside lookup, it must state *what* the finding is, *why* it is stakes-class, the *evidence* behind it, and name a timing tier that is exactly one of `end-gate-expanded` (the default, held for the human end-gate) or `mid-flight` (the narrow immediate tier). Fifth, the two paths must be mutually exclusive for a single finding — never both a committed fix and an escalation. Sixth, a finding the agent judges not worth fixing must be *dismissed with a non-empty written rationale*, never an empty one. And finally, the existing green-field build mode must be untouched — a normal story still builds exactly as before, with no escalation and no disposition output.

How it was checked. A QA agent verified the built files against each of those required behaviors, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for ways the implementation could violate them; because this item carries genuine stakes (it is the gate that decides what gets silently committed versus raised to a human), it was re-checked after fixes were applied. Be clear about the strength of that verification: the deliverables here are markdown agent-definition and workflow files (`dev.md` and `dev/workflow.md`), and the runtime harness for this story is currently "skip" — so nothing was *executed*. Verification was done by structured inspection of the written behavior against the frozen contract, not by actually invoking the dev agent on live routine and stakes-class payloads and observing the commits and escalations it produces. The behavior is specified precisely and reads as correct, but "verified" here means "inspected and matched the contract," not "run and observed."

Result. The verdict is PASS, recorded at round 4 with the freeze marked `MATCH` (the implementation matches the frozen contract scope) and the post-fix re-check marked `CLEAN`. Five findings were raised across the loop and all five were fixed (zero escalated, zero dismissed). The load-bearing fix was a HIGH-severity one: an earlier draft would have escalated a stakes-class finding *regardless of whether it was legitimate*, which the reviewer caught and corrected so that escalation is now gated on `legitimate: true` — a non-legitimate stakes-class finding is dismissed (with rationale), never escalated. The remaining fixes aligned the vocabulary (field names like `description`/`finding_id`) to the shared finding-schema and confirmed the `triaged-out` path is reachable. The item is merged.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. Rather than building a separate "fixer" agent, this story teaches the existing dev agent a second operating mode. Dev now runs in one of two modes selected purely by its input: if the input carries a `directed_fix` payload it enters fix-mode; anything else is the unchanged green-field build. The heart of fix-mode is a single branch on the finding's `stakes_class`. Routine findings stay on the fast, silent always-auto-fix path — dev edits the file and commits. Only the non-routine classes (security/auth-isolation, irreversible/destructive, high-blast-radius/architecture) peel off that path: dev makes zero edits, zero commits, and instead returns a self-contained escalation payload (what / why / evidence / timing tier) for the Conductor to surface. This branch is the entire point of the story — without it, the `stakes_class` field added by the upstream contract would be inert data that changes no behavior.

Why it's built this way. This is the dividing line that keeps the conduct model honest. DEC-035 establishes conduct as *one human gate at the end* with a legible auto-fix loop and no firehose of mid-flight interruptions — its binding decision #1 says routine findings are auto-fixed silently. DEC-036 then narrowly amends that decision to permit a high-bar escalation tier so genuinely dangerous findings are not silently committed and buried; its amendment D2 is exactly this stakes-class branch in fix-mode, and DEC-036 also adds the `escalated` disposition, requires a non-empty rationale on `dismissed`, and defines the two timing tiers (`end-gate-expanded` default, `mid-flight` narrow). The implementation holds the line on both sides: if dev escalated everything, conduct would become the firehose DEC-035 was designed to eliminate; if dev silently fixed everything, a stakes-class finding would be lost in the noise — so the branch escalates *only* the narrow stakes-class set and keeps the routine path untouched. The mid-flight tier is deliberately kept narrow (irreversible-and-imminent OR build-invalidating only; urgency or stakes class alone is not enough), and dev never pauses or prompts the human itself — it only emits the tier flag and lets the Conductor own surfacing, preserving the single-owner / single-end-gate model.

What changed in the diff below. Two markdown files: `skills/momentum/agents/dev.md` — the agent definition now declares the two modes, the fix-mode input shape, the stakes-class routing rules, the escalation payload, the dismissed-rationale requirement, and the green-field no-op guarantees. And `skills/momentum/skills/dev/workflow.md` — the procedural workflow adds mode detection (Step 0) and the fix-mode execution path (Step 0.5) that applies the stakes-class branch finding-by-finding and emits the per-finding disposition map. No application, UI, or backend code is touched; this is an `agent-definition` change.

The automatic fix loopstage3-fix-loop-via-directed-dev  had a high-risk divergence → §03

This story builds the per-story fix loop: after a piece of work is reviewed, ordinary problems get fixed and re-checked automatically (up to three tries) with no human in the way, while a narrow class of genuinely high-stakes problems (security holes, irreversible changes like data migrations or force-pushes) are pulled out of the auto-fix path and raised to a human instead of being quietly changed. The guarantee it gives the conduct system is that routine issues fix themselves cheaply while dangerous ones can never be silently auto-corrected and slipped past; without this piece, an auto-fixer could spin forever, ship work it never actually repaired, or silently fix a security or destructive change a human should have seen.

Delivered: The system now has a working per-story fix loop that auto-resolves ordinary findings within a bounded three-try retry, marks genuinely stuck work as blocked instead of shipping it, and routes high-stakes findings onto a separate escalation channel that surfaces them to a human rather than fixing them silently.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. This item is the per-story "fix loop" — the part of the autonomous build that takes the problems QA and code-review found, hands them to a fixer, re-checks, and repeats until the story is clean. The frozen contract demanded six distinct behaviors. (1) A run-of-the-mill ("routine") finding must get a fix applied, be re-checked, and resolve as `fixed` — with no human ever prompted about it. (2) A routine finding the fixer genuinely cannot solve must stop after at most three fix-and-recheck attempts, be marked BLOCKED, and the build must move on to the next story instead of halting the whole run. (3) A finding that warrants escalation must be recorded with a separate disposition, `escalated` — clearly not "fixed," not "still remaining," and not BLOCKED — and must be pulled out of the retry loop so it is never silently fixed. (4) By default an escalated finding rides to the single human end-gate as a decision-card item. (5) Only the two narrow cases — a finding that is irreversible-and-imminent OR build-invalidating — may trigger a mid-build raise instead of waiting for the end-gate; nothing else may. (6) A high-stakes finding (auth-isolation regression, a destructive migration / force-push / prod deploy, a high-blast-radius architecture change) must never be quietly auto-fixed inside the loop — it must always be routed to the escalation channel and made visible. The contract also required that escalating one finding must not disrupt the normal handling of routine findings on other items.

How it was checked — and how strong that check is. A QA agent verified the built workflow text against each of those required behaviors, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for ways the routing could leak or break. Because this item is high-risk (it carries the core DEC-036 guarantee that high-stakes work is never silently changed), it went through re-checks after fixes rather than a single look. Be clear-eyed about what "verified" means here: the deliverable is a markdown skill-instruction file (the Conductor's workflow), and the runtime harness for this sprint is set to skip — so nothing was executed against a live build. Verification was done by structured inspection of the workflow logic against the frozen contract, not by running it. The reviewers traced each branch of the prose (the `fixed` / `dismissed` / `triaged-out` / `escalated` / `blocked` cases, the retry counter, the mid-flight dispatch, the stakes-class guard) and confirmed the written behavior matches what the contract requires. They did not observe a real story being fixed.

Result — PASS. The final verdict was PASS with the contract fingerprint matching the frozen spec (freeze: MATCH). Of six findings raised during review, five were fixed and one was dismissed (correctly: it flagged that the stage-1/stage-2 call sites are still hollow, which is expected — other stories own wiring those). The most important catch was a HIGH-severity bug: BLOCKED stories were being merged anyway, contradicting the governing spec's rule that a blocked story must be "left unmerged." The fix now removes a blocked story from the run without merging it and spins a triage stub instead — and the re-check confirmed the fix held (recheck: REFIXED). The re-check also corrected an invalid status transition and a dangling reference, and confirmed the mid-flight escalation follows a single-classifier contract (the Conductor hands the whole batch to one escalation engine rather than pre-judging the bar itself). One honesty note carried in the diff: the end-gate-escalations list is written and emitted but not yet consumed by the end-gate report — that wiring is explicitly marked as a downstream story's job, so it is a traceable hand-off, not a silent dead-end.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. The fix loop is built as a single per-story step (`2.S3`) with two clearly separated lanes. The routine lane is the unchanged, always-on default: fix → re-check → loop, capped at three attempts, then BLOCKED-and-continue. Bolted onto that baseline is the escalation lane: any finding the fixer returns as `escalated` is yanked out of the retry loop immediately and pushed onto an escalation channel rather than being fixed. This design was chosen so the common case stays fully autonomous and quiet — the vast majority of findings never touch a human — while a narrow, high-bar tier of genuinely consequential findings is forced into the open. The Conductor stays the sole authority that commits code; the fixer is a subagent that only proposes fixes and never mutates git itself, which keeps the autonomous loop legible and auditable.

Why it is built this way — the governing decisions. Per the story's References and Dev Notes, two decisions drive this. DEC-035 adopted "conduct" as the execution engine with exactly one human gate at the end, no story-count cap, and a hard requirement that the auto-fix loop remain legible — that is why routine findings are always auto-fixed silently and the build never stops between stories on the routine path. DEC-036 narrowly amends DEC-035's binding decision #1: routine findings stay always-auto-fixed, but a high-bar, stakes-gated tier of findings must *leave* the silent auto-fix path so they are raised, never silently changed. This is why `escalated` exists as a first-class disposition and why high-stakes findings are guarded against silent fixing. The implementation cites the governing spec's section 3 (Phase B–D, the per-story fix loop) and section 4 (the fix-loop mechanics: retry bound and BLOCKED-then-continue) as the leg it wires. It also honors the "three-place absolute" from DEC-036: the "ALWAYS auto-fix" rule lives in three spots, and this story threads the stage-3 leg specifically — the stakes-class guard here ensures that even though the other two legs are wired by sibling stories, a high-stakes finding can never slip through this one. The default-to-end-gate, mid-flight-only-for-two-narrow-conditions design reflects DEC-036's deliberate bias: the end-gate is the safety net, and the mid-build interrupt stays rare.

What changed in the diff. A single file: `skills/momentum/skills/conductor/workflow.md`, specifically the new step `2.S3` (the stage-3 per-story fix loop) plus its supporting notes (the supervision invariant, the routine-path guarantee, the escalation-path guard, and the isolation invariant). What you are reviewing below is the prose logic of that loop — the disposition branches, the three-attempt retry bound, the BLOCKED-leave-unmerged behavior, and the stakes/timing escalation routing.
