"blocked" is not a valid state in the tool's state machine — "closed-incomplete" is the correct durable terminal status (consistent with the exhausted-retries branch at step 2.2).+            Spin a triage stub for the blocked findings: invoke momentum:triage with the blocked findings list for S, so they are queued into the backlog.+            Record in {{build_log}}: { slug: S.slug, event: "stage3-story-blocked", leftover_count: length(blocked findings), note: "story left unmerged per spec §3" }+            Continue building remaining stories in {{running}} and {{frontier}}.+          </action>+        </check>++      </check>++    </step>+     <!-- ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────── -->     <!-- STEP 2.2 — Heartbeat: react to each terminal signal     -->     <!-- ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────── -->

The Conductor — the engine that runs a whole build

The Conductor skeleton + spineconduct-skill-scaffold-and-spine  had a high-risk divergence → §03

This story builds the backbone of the whole autonomous build system: the top-level skill that runs an entire sprint's worth of stories end to end and the ordered script of phases it follows. Its guarantee is that one and only one place is in charge of changing the codebase and launching helper agents, that the phases always run in the right order, and that the developer is asked nothing during a normal build until a single review at the very end. Without this piece there is no orchestrator at all — nothing sequences the build, no one owns the working tree, and every other part of conduct (the per-story builds, the auto-fix loop, the escalation path, the final report) has nothing to attach to.

Delivered: A working orchestrator skill now exists that drives a full sprint through pre-flight, per-story builds, post-merge review, end-to-end validation, and a single closing review — with all code-changing and agent-launching authority concentrated in one accountable place and no interruptions to the developer along the way.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. The frozen contract for this item set six behaviors the built skill had to satisfy. First, the Conductor skill must actually load and present itself as the top-level, in-session build orchestrator for a whole sprint — clean frontmatter, no missing files, no parse errors. Second, its workflow must lay out the build as one ordered five-phase spine — pre-flight, then the build phase, then validation-on-merge, then end-to-end validation, then a single human acceptance gate — with no later phase ever appearing before an earlier one. Third, on a routine run the orchestrator must ask the developer nothing between starting the run and that final gate: exactly two touchpoints on the normal path (run start and end gate), and never a between-stories interruption. Fourth, the build phase must impose no story-count cap — a big sprint is handled by repeating the per-story unit, not by refusing or truncating. Fifth, the workflow text must delegate all the real work: every bit of code/spec/fix authoring goes to a spawned helper (the orchestrator writes nothing inline), helpers are told not to change git history on their own, and spawning is single-tier (helpers don't spawn further build helpers). Sixth, there must be an explicit pause-ask-resume escalation path, framed as a narrow, high-bar exception for a small stakes-class of findings — with the supervision rule worded as "never asks during build except the narrow stakes-and-timing mid-flight escalation tier" rather than an absolute no-questions claim, and routine findings explicitly barred from that path. And the single human acceptance gate must be the only one, sitting after end-to-end validation.

How it was checked — and how strong that check is. A QA agent walked the built `SKILL.md` and `workflow.md` against each of those six required behaviors, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted the same files for ways they could fail. The code-review gate is recorded as "pass-after-fix," meaning the reviewer found real problems and the item was re-checked after they were fixed. Be clear-eyed about the nature of this verification, though: these deliverables are markdown instruction files (a skill and its workflow spine), and the runtime harness for this story is "skill-invoke," which in this build was not exercised as a live execution — the orchestrator was not actually run end-to-end against a real multi-story sprint. So every claim above ("loads cleanly," "asks nothing between stories," "produces commits in history") was confirmed by structured reading of the instruction text against the frozen contract, not by observing the orchestrator run. "Verified" here means the spine says the right things in the right order, not that we watched it do them. Two scenarios in the contract literally call for a completed run producing artifacts and commits; those were judged by what the workflow instructs, not by an executed run.

Result. The recorded verdict is PASS, with the build result attesting the frozen contract was an exact match (no scope drift in what was verified) and the QA evidence noting "all scenarios satisfied by structural reading of conductor SKILL.md + workflow.md." Three findings were raised and all three were judged legitimate and fixed (none escalated, none dismissed). The substantive fix is worth the reader's eye in the diff: the reviewer caught two places where the spine had inserted developer HALTs — a per-story rebase HALT and an end-gate merge HALT — which would have violated the very "one gate, no mid-run questions" contract this story exists to establish; both were removed so the Conductor resolves and retries those conflicts itself, and a push-confirmation step was added to the touchpoint enumeration. The item merged. One informational note carries no risk: the model is declared as the full ID `claude-opus-4-6`, which matches the repo's convention.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. This item is the keystone of the "conduct" leg — the in-session, single-end-gate rewrite of the legacy story-by-story `momentum:sprint-dev`. Rather than spreading build responsibility across many skills that each ask the developer something, it concentrates the entire build phase into one accountable orchestrator (the Conductor) and one ordered workflow spine. The Conductor is the sole authority for two things that are dangerous to let subagents do independently — mutating git and spawning agents — and it writes no code, spec, or fix itself; all of that is delegated. Centralizing git mutation keeps the build auditable and stops parallel subagents from racing each other on the working tree; centralizing spawning to a single tier keeps the agent graph shallow and predictable. The whole sprint runs to a single human gate at the very end, so the developer engages at a small, defined set of touchpoints instead of being interrupted story by story.

Why it's built this way — the governing decisions. Per the story's References and Dev Notes, two decisions drive this design. DEC-035 adopts conduct in the first place: one human gate at the end, no story-count cap, a report organized by user-facing functionality, and a legible auto-fix loop. That is the source of the five-phase spine, the no-cap build phase, and the single end-gate-last requirement. DEC-036 amends DEC-035's first binding decision by carving out a narrow, high-bar, stakes-gated mid-flight escalation tier — so the build can pause and ask mid-flight, but only for a small stakes-class of findings (irreversible-and-imminent or build-invalidating), while routine findings stay always auto-fixed and silent. That is why the spine reads "never asks during build EXCEPT the narrow stakes-and-timing mid-flight escalation tier" rather than asserting an absolute no-questions rule — an absolute claim would contradict DEC-036 and leave the escalation branch with no defined home. Deliberately, this story only lays the *structural* pause-ask-resume branch and softens the invariant wording; it does not implement the logic that decides which findings qualify (that selection engine is a separate downstream story). The governing conduct spec sections cited are section 3 (the Conductor's authority surface and the repeated per-story pipeline unit), section 2 (the end-to-end flow the phase sequence must mirror), and section 8 (the single end-gate).

What changed — what you're looking at in the diff. Two new files under the new skill directory: `skills/momentum/skills/conductor/SKILL.md` (the orchestrator skill — frontmatter, the four authority/restraint invariants, the softened supervision invariant, and a pointer to the spine) and `skills/momentum/skills/conductor/workflow.md` (the executable spine that sequences pre-flight → build phase of per-story pipelines → AVFL-on-merge → E2E → single end-gate, with the delegation and single-tier-spawning instructions and the structural-only escalation branch). The keystone is intentionally a skeleton: the per-story pipeline internals, the auto-fix loop, the escalation decision engine, and the anti-rubber-stamp end-gate are left hollow for later stories in the leg to fill.

The build schedulerconduct-build-phase-frontier  had a high-risk divergence → §03

This story builds the engine that runs an entire sprint's work autonomously: it figures out which work items have all their prerequisites met, launches all of them at once with no ceiling, and keeps the run moving as each item finishes, so the whole sprint builds in one continuous flow with a single human checkpoint at the very end. It also carries a narrow safety valve: if a work item hits a genuinely serious, can't-wait problem (something irreversible-and-about-to-happen, or something that breaks the whole build), that one branch pauses and raises it to a human instead of quietly pushing on. Without this piece, a single failed item would strand the rest of the sprint, the run would stall and need babysitting, and a serious problem could slip through unnoticed.

Delivered: The Conductor can now drive a full sprint from ready to merged on its own: it launches all unblocked work concurrently, relaunches newly-unblocked work the moment something merges, survives a failed item without halting the whole run, and pauses-and-escalates only the rare problems that clear a deliberately narrow seriousness bar.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. This item is the engine of the autonomous build — the loop that drives a sprint's stories from "ready" to "merged" with no human standing in the middle. The frozen contract demanded six concrete behaviors. First, launch everything that's ready, all at once, with no cap: every story whose dependencies have all reached "review" or beyond must launch concurrently, and a fixture with more stories than any plausible batch size (eight-plus) must show all of them launching together — none held back merely because many were ready. Second, react to merges without a human prompt: when a story merges, the stories it was blocking must launch immediately, with no manual step between. Third, never stall on one failure: a story rigged to fail through all its retries must be marked blocked while every other story keeps advancing to a normal finish. Fourth, the narrow mid-flight escalation: when a story's pipeline surfaces a finding that meets the strict bar — irreversible-and-imminent OR build-invalidating — that one branch must pause and hand off to the escalation path (raised, not silently auto-fixed), while the other branches keep running. Fifth, the anti-widening guard: a stakes finding that does *not* clear that narrow bar must NOT pause mid-flight — it rides through to the end-gate instead. Sixth, the anti-firehose guard: an ordinary routine finding must never pause anything; the run continues normally.

How it was checked — and the honest limits of that. A QA agent verified the built workflow text against each of those required behaviors, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for bugs; because this item was high-risk, it was re-checked after fixes were applied (the record shows it landed on the third round). Be clear about what "verified" means here: the deliverable is a markdown skill workflow file (`conductor/workflow.md`), and the skill-invoke runtime harness for this sprint is not wired up — so verification was done by structured inspection of the workflow instructions against the frozen contract, not by running a live sprint fixture through the loop and watching it behave. The reviewers confirmed the instructions *say* the right things in the right places (no-count-cap launch at step 2.1, the event-driven heartbeat at step 2.2, the bounded-retry-then-continue handling, and the consumption hook at step 2.F that defers detection/classification to the escalation engine rather than re-implementing it). What was not done is an end-to-end execution proving the loop actually launches eight pipelines at once or actually pauses one branch on a real stakes finding. Treat this as a strong contract-conformance read of the spec text, not as proof the runtime behaves.

Result. PASS. The build record for this item reports the QA verdict as PASS with the frozen contract matching (no drift between what was specified and what was built), five findings raised and all five fixed, zero escalated and zero dismissed, and the post-fix re-check confirming the fixes held. The concrete findings that were fixed: frontier worktree-creation handling, resume behavior when a blocker is already merged (the "merged-seed" case), handling of dependents that are blocked, a backward-status-transition `--force` correction caught in the re-check, and a clean auto-merge of the result into the workflow file's Phase 2. The single deliverable merged cleanly: `skills/momentum/skills/conductor/workflow.md` (the Phase 2 frontier).

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. The build phase is written as an event-driven dependency frontier rather than the legacy fixed-size waves. The Conductor reads the sprint's story set and each story's `depends_on`, computes the set of stories whose blockers have all reached "review" or beyond, and launches that whole frontier concurrently as per-story pipelines. It then sits in a heartbeat: each time a pipeline reports a terminal signal (`merged` or `failed`), it reacts — on `merged` it re-evaluates the frontier and launches whatever just became unblocked; on exhausted retries it marks that one story blocked and keeps going rather than halting the run. This was chosen because the legacy `momentum:sprint-dev` builds in discrete waves with a human gate between them, which fragments long sprints into stop-and-wait segments and forces the developer to babysit the run. A non-halting frontier is what makes "one human gate at the very end" actually achievable — without it, any single story failure would strand the rest of the sprint and drag the human back into the middle of the flow.

Why the consumption hook, not a detector. Layered on top is a deliberately narrow mid-flight escalation consumption hook (step 2.F). The frontier itself does *not* detect or classify findings — it defers entirely to the escalation engine and only consumes the engine's outcome, then either pauses that one branch or continues. The bar to pause is kept strict on purpose (irreversible-and-imminent OR build-invalidating, and nothing wider); routine findings never reach the hook and stay on the always-auto-fix path, and stakes findings that miss the narrow bar are carried to the end-gate rather than widening the trigger. This keeps a pure "never halt, always auto-fix" engine from being too absolute, without re-opening the firehose of mid-build interruptions the design exists to prevent.

Governing decisions. This item is grounded directly in DEC-035 and DEC-036, as the story's Description, Dev Notes, and References cite. DEC-035 adopts the Conductor model — one human gate at the end, no story-count cap (its D4 invariant, honored as the absolute no-cap launch at step 2.1), and a legible auto-fix loop. DEC-036 narrowly amends DEC-035's first binding decision to permit the high-bar, stakes-gated mid-flight escalation tier (its D1/D2) — which is exactly what the consumption hook consumes while leaving the routine always-auto-fix path of DEC-035 untouched. The grounding spec sections the story names are Section 3 (per-story dependency gating, frontier re-evaluation, the `>= review` readiness gate, and the bounded-retry/continue-don't-halt behavior — the primary basis for AC 1–5) and Section 2 (the overall Conductor spine the build phase plugs into: the event-driven session loop and terminal-signal contract).

What changed in the diff below. One file: `skills/momentum/skills/conductor/workflow.md` — specifically its Phase 2 (the build phase). The diff adds the frontier-state initialization (step 2.0), the no-cap concurrent launch (step 2.1), the event-driven heartbeat that reacts to terminal signals (step 2.2), and the mid-flight escalation consumption hook (step 2.F) that routes through the separate escalation engine spec. Read it as workflow *instructions* — markdown that tells the Conductor what to do — not as executable code.

The stop-and-ask engineconduct-stakes-timing-escalation-mechanism  had a high-risk divergence → §03

This piece is the one and only place in conduct that is allowed to stop an autonomous build mid-run and ask the developer a question — but only for a tiny, dangerous class of moments: an action that is about to do something irreversible (a destructive database migration, a delete, a force-push, a production deploy) or that would break the build itself. Everything else stays silent and gets collapsed into the single end-of-run report. Without this piece, conduct would have only two bad options: interrupt the developer constantly (the "firehose" it was designed to avoid) or never interrupt at all — meaning a destructive, irreversible action could fire automatically with no chance for the developer to stop it first.

Delivered: Conduct now has a single shared "pause and ask" mechanism that interrupts the build exactly once for each genuinely irreversible-and-imminent or build-breaking finding — presenting what changed, why it matters, and the evidence inline — lets the developer answer proceed / change / abort-that-branch, and resumes; everything below that bar continues to run untouched.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. The frozen contract held this engine to six behaviors, all variations on a single discipline: interrupt the developer rarely, and only for the right reasons. First, a build full of ordinary work must run start-to-finish with zero mid-flight questions — a routine finding and a "real but not about-to-happen" stakes finding both have to flow silently into the final report, never as a live pause. Second, a finding that is genuinely irreversible and imminent (an imminent destructive migration, a delete, a force-push, a prod deploy) must stop the build with exactly one pause, raised *before* the action happens. Third, the same single-pause behavior must fire for a finding that would invalidate the build — while a non-imminent stakes finding in a parallel run stays silent, proving the engine discriminates on the narrow bar and not merely on "this looks important." Fourth, that one pause must be self-sufficient: it has to state what the change is, why it matters, and show the evidence inline, so the developer can decide without leaving the surface to go look anything up. Fifth, the developer must be able to answer in exactly three ways — proceed, change, or abort-that-branch — and the build must resume correctly for each, never hanging and never re-asking a resolved finding. Sixth, raised findings must be recorded under a distinct escalated disposition, visibly separate from fixed, dismissed, and triaged-out. The contract also named the explicit failure mode: a run with many non-qualifying findings and only two that clear the bar must produce exactly two pauses — no firehose.

How it was checked. A QA agent verified the built files against each of those required behaviors, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for bugs; because this item was flagged HIGH-risk (it is the escalation engine itself), it was re-checked after fixes were applied. You should read "verified" precisely here: the deliverables are markdown skill and reference files (a workflow specification and a behavioral reference), and the runtime harness for this sprint is `skill-invoke` operating by structured inspection against the frozen contract — not live execution. No Conductor build was actually run over crafted findings to observe the pauses fire. What was confirmed is that the written specification, read against the contract scenario-by-scenario, mandates each required behavior — the bar logic, the single-surface pause, the three resolution branches, the escalated disposition, and the anti-firehose count guarantee are all present and internally consistent. That is a strong reading of intent and wiring; it is not a demonstration of the behavior at runtime.

Result. The verdict is PASS with a clean freeze MATCH (the build satisfied the contract that was frozen at assignment, with no drift). Five findings were surfaced during the build and all five were fixed — including a HIGH where an `escalation_reason` field had been invented that did not exist in the canonical finding schema (reconciled to consume the schema's existing `mid-flight` timing value instead), and a vocabulary mismatch where an older Continue/Defer answer set was corrected to the contract's proceed / change / abort-branch. The post-fix re-check ("REFIXED") also repaired a dangling pointer in the workflow's step 2.F and a single-vs-array return-shape inconsistency. Zero findings on this item were themselves escalated or dismissed. The two deliverables exist on disk as recorded — `skills/momentum/skills/conductor/references/escalation.md` and the step 2.F wiring in `skills/momentum/skills/conductor/workflow.md` — and the merge landed on the sprint branch.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. This engine is the one place in the Conductor that is allowed to interrupt an otherwise-autonomous build, and the design deliberately concentrates that power into a single shared primitive rather than scattering it. The Conductor's default posture is silence: it runs every story to completion and surfaces a single human gate at the end. This story carves out the *only* sanctioned exception — a narrow, high-bar pause that fires before an irreversible-and-imminent or build-invalidating action, and for nothing else. The key design move is that detection-of-the-bar and the pause mechanism live here *once*. The in-flight build leg (`conduct-build-phase-frontier`) and the merge/conflict leg both route their mid-flight escalation decisions through this engine instead of each growing its own pause prompt. That is intentional: if either caller re-implemented the bar, the narrow bar would fragment and inevitably drift wider over time. Keeping it singular is how the narrowness is defended structurally, not just by convention. The engine also stays in its lane — it does not define the stakes-class schema field (it consumes it from the canonical finding schema), it does not implement the end-gate, and it does not own the anti-rubber-stamp forcing function. It only consumes already-classified findings, applies the bar, raises the one pause, and stamps the escalated disposition.

Why it's built this way — the governing decisions. The whole shape of this engine is dictated by DEC-036, which narrowly amends DEC-035. DEC-035 established conduct's foundation: adopt the Conductor as the execution engine, with exactly *one* human gate at the end and an auto-fix loop that must remain legible. DEC-036 then introduced the single, deliberately narrow exception this story realizes. Specifically: DEC-036 D1 defines the two-tier timing model — tier (a) end-gate-expanded as the default safety net, and tier (b) mid-flight as the narrow interruption — and sets the bar for tier (b) as *irreversible-and-imminent OR build-invalidating and nothing else*; this engine is tier (b). DEC-036 D2 supplies the stakes finding-classes the engine reads (security/auth-isolation, irreversible/destructive, high-blast-radius/architecture, and default routine). DEC-036 D5 mandates the self-sufficiency floor — every escalation surface must carry what/why/evidence inline — which is why the pause-ask is specified to be decidable in place. DEC-036 D4 is explicitly *out of scope* here: the anti-rubber-stamp forcing function lives in the report and end-gate, and this engine only feeds the `escalated` disposition into that downstream legibility. And the DEC-036 Decision Gate names the failure mode the whole design guards against — over-escalation re-creating the firehose that DEC-035's "one end-gate" rule existed to prevent — which is the source of the engine's explicit "bias narrow, never widen the bar" guard. The conduct spec sections cited by the story (§3, §7, §8 plumbing for mid-flight routing; §14 stakes-class table) carry these decisions into the spec the engine implements.

What changed in the diff below. Two files. `skills/momentum/skills/conductor/references/escalation.md` is new — the engine's behavioral specification: its inputs (stakes class and timing tier off a per-story result), the narrow bar, the single-surface pause-ask with its what/why/evidence contract, the three resolution outcomes and their resume behavior, the escalated disposition, and the anti-firehose guard. `skills/momentum/skills/conductor/workflow.md` is edited to wire the engine into the build loop at step 2.F (the mid-flight escalation consumption hook) and step 2.S3 (the per-story fix loop that routes stakes-class findings to escalation rather than silently auto-fixing them), and to route the merge leg through the same engine. Reviewing the diff, the load-bearing thing to check is that the bar in both files reads exactly "irreversible-and-imminent OR build-invalidating" with no additional conditions, and that the resolution vocabulary is consistently proceed / change / abort-that-branch — both were defects caught and fixed during the build.

The run is not left hung.++---++## Escalated Disposition++Any finding raised mid-flight via this engine is recorded with the **`escalated`** disposition.++**Disposition vocabulary:**++| Disposition | Producer | Meaning |+|---|---|---|+| `fixed` | Routine auto-fix path inside the pipeline | Silently resolved; developer not involved |+| `dismissed` | Auto-fix loop (requires non-empty rationale) | Waved off by the fixer; rationale recorded |+| `triaged-out` | Auto-fix loop | Outside scope; not actioned |+| `escalated` | This engine only | Raised mid-flight to the developer; not silently fixed |++`escalated` is the sole producer of the `escalated` disposition. No other path produces it. Per-story merge + conflict handlingconduct-merge-and-conflict-resolution  had a high-risk divergence → §03

This piece owns how each finished work item gets folded into the combined result: it rebases and merges every story, quietly fixes the conflicts it safely can, sets aside the ones it can't (preserving the work so nothing is lost), and — critically — stops and pulls the developer in only when fixing a conflict would touch something dangerous (a security/login boundary, an irreversible action like a delete or a database migration) or break the build. Without it, a single bad merge would either halt the whole run, silently discard a story's work, or — worst of all — silently rewrite something dangerous and ship it with nobody ever being asked.

Delivered: The conduct system can now integrate every story autonomously — auto-fixing safe conflicts, quarantining unmergeable ones without stopping the sprint, and raising a mid-flight alarm to the developer (and only for) the narrow set of conflict fixes that would alter a security boundary, do something irreversible, or break the build.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. The frozen contract for this item pinned down six behaviors the Conductor's per-story merge path must exhibit, and they map cleanly onto the question "does one bad merge ever stop the sprint, and does a dangerous resolution ever slip through silently?" In plain terms: (1) a *trivial* conflict — an additive, non-overlapping collision the machinery can reconcile mechanically — must integrate cleanly with zero operator prompts and zero escalation records. (2) A *semantic* conflict that touches no security/auth area and doesn't break the build — overlapping edits to the same logic — must be resolved autonomously: the Conductor fires its fixer, retries the merge, lands the story, and never pulls the developer in. (3) A story that simply cannot be reconciled must, after a bounded number of attempts (not an infinite loop), be quarantined — its work preserved, its conflict detail recorded — while the *other* stories keep integrating and the sprint runs to its end-gate rather than halting. (4) A resolution whose only viable fix would cross an auth/security boundary, do something irreversible (migration, delete, force-push, prod deploy), or break the build must be raised mid-flight and recorded as an *escalation* — explicitly distinct from a clean auto-fix record and from a quarantine record. (5) When a sprint contains *both* a routine semantic resolution and a stakes/build-invalidating one, only the latter may be raised — the narrow bar has to hold, with exactly one escalation produced. (6) The final sprint-to-main merge must not trigger a mid-flight raise on its own; it stays inside the operator-approved end-gate sequence.

How it was checked, and how strong that check is. This deliverable is a markdown skill workflow — instructions the Conductor follows, not compiled code — and its declared verification method is `skill-invoke`. The runtime harness that would actually drive a live conduct sprint through staged trivial/semantic/unmergeable/stakes conflicts is currently skipped, so be clear-eyed about what "verified" means here: this was checked by structured inspection of the workflow text against the frozen contract, not by live execution. A QA agent read the built step 2.2.M against each of the six required behaviors, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for control-flow bugs — paths where the spec *says* the right thing but the actual step ordering would do the wrong thing. That adversarial pass is what gives this item its credibility, because it caught real defects rather than rubber-stamping prose (see below). No behavior was confirmed by observing the Conductor actually merge a conflicted branch; the confidence rests on the logic being internally consistent and matching the contract, which is weaker than execution.

Result — PASS, and it earned it. The recorded verdict is PASS with the frozen contract reported as a MATCH (the built item was checked against the same contract that was frozen at assignment, not a drifted one). The evidence is the substantive part: the review surfaced 6 findings, all 6 fixed, including two HIGH-severity control-flow bugs that would have broken the contract had they shipped — the quarantine path "fell through" into the success block (a quarantined, unmergeable story would have been wrongly recorded as merged), and the escalation hook was written as a *passive note* rather than an executable step (the stakes/build-invalidating raise would never actually fire). Because the item was high-risk, it was re-checked after the fixes (recorded as REFIXED), and that recheck caught two further defects: an unconditional `--abort` that was discarding a valid resolution, and a contradiction between the abort and triage paths. Zero findings were escalated or dismissed; all were repaired. The fixed workflow now routes quarantine to its own terminal path that explicitly never reaches the success block, and the escalation hook (step 2.2.M.4.E) is an executable action with an explicit narrow-bar gate. The item was merged. The honest summary: the *logic* is well-verified against the contract by inspection and survived an adversarial pass plus a recheck, but it has not been exercised live.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. This story makes the Conductor — the single top-level session that owns the build phase — the *sole* writer of git history for every per-story merge. No spawned subagent ever rebases, merges, or commits; subagents (including the directed fixer) produce *content*, and the Conductor performs the actual git operations. For each completed story it runs a rebase step followed by a merge step, both conflict-guarded, into the sprint integration branch. Conflicts are sorted into two kinds: *trivial* (mechanically reconcilable — non-overlapping hunks, additive collisions, deterministic index/lockfile churn) which auto-resolve silently, and *semantic* (the fix requires understanding intent) which fire a directed fixer and retry. Retries are bounded at 3; a story that still won't integrate is quarantined — branch preserved, conflict recorded, sprint continues. This design exists to kill a specific failure mode: the legacy model treated any merge conflict as a stop-the-world event that pulled the human in and stalled the sprint. Quarantine-and-continue replaces HALT, so one unmergeable story can never halt the whole run.

Why the escalation hook exists — DEC-035 and DEC-036. The governing tension is between two decisions. DEC-035 (adopt conduct; single human end-gate; no story-count cap; legible auto-fix loop) established the anti-firehose posture: by binding decision #1, conflict resolutions should be auto-resolved and the sprint should just keep going. That's right for the common case but dangerous when a semantic resolution would silently rewrite an auth boundary or break the build for every downstream story. DEC-036 amends DEC-035 #1 *narrowly*: it introduces a stakes-gated mid-flight escalation tier and makes `escalated` (raised, not silently fixed) a first-class disposition alongside `fixed`, `dismissed`, and `triaged-out`. So this story's merge path stays autonomous by default but escalates when — and only when — a semantic resolution would touch a stakes class (security/auth-isolation; irreversible/destructive; high-blast-radius/architecture) or is build-invalidating. That narrow bar is load-bearing and the spec states explicitly it must never widen: routine semantic resolutions auto-continue with no pause. The governing spec section is Section 6 (the Conductor owns ALL git mutation: per-story rebase + merge, conflict detection, trivial auto-resolution, fixer-driven semantic resolution with retry bound 3, and quarantine-not-HALT). A deliberate scope nuance from DEC-036 is also encoded: the terminal sprint-to-main merge is not a mid-flight trigger — it already lives inside the developer-gated end-gate APPROVE sequence, so the hook explicitly refuses to fire for it. The escalation hook itself is a structural *seam*: this story owns the hook's existence and call site (step 2.2.M.4.E), while the sibling story `conduct-stakes-timing-escalation-mechanism` owns the stakes-classification logic and the shared pause primitive (step 2.F) that the hook calls.

What the diff changes. A single file: `skills/momentum/skills/conductor/workflow.md`. The added machinery is the step 2.2.M block — `2.2.M.0` initializes the per-story `merge_attempts` counter (bound 3), `2.2.M.1` and `2.2.M.2` are the conflict-guarded rebase and merge steps, `2.2.M.3` classifies trivial vs. semantic, `2.2.M.4` is the abort-and-bounded-retry gate, `2.2.M.4.E` is the narrow-bar escalation hook into the shared engine, `2.2.M.5` is the quarantine-not-HALT terminal path, and `2.2.M.6` is the successful-integration path that records the `escalated` disposition for any raised resolution. The reader looking at the diff below should focus on those sub-steps and the way the quarantine and escalation paths are kept structurally separate from the success path — that separation is exactly what the two HIGH-severity fixes restored.

Tamper check on the test contractconduct-contract-freeze-check  had a high-risk divergence → §03

This piece guarantees that when conduct checks whether a story passed or failed, it checks against the exact same test criteria that were agreed on and locked in at the start of the build — not a version that was edited, regenerated, or quietly drifted in between. Without it, a story could "pass" against criteria nobody approved, or "fail" against criteria it was never given, and the final report the developer trusts would be built on a result that was silently scored against the wrong yardstick.

Delivered: Conduct now takes a fingerprint of each story's test criteria when work begins, re-checks that fingerprint right before scoring the story, and refuses to record a result for any story whose criteria changed underneath it — handling the mismatch itself without ever interrupting the developer.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. The frozen contract pinned five behaviors the freeze check must exhibit, all observable by running the Conductor — never by reading its source. (1) Unchanged contract passes: when a story's contract file is byte-for-byte what it was when the story was assigned, the Conductor verifies the story normally and the build is not interrupted. (2) Drifted contract is caught: if the contract file was altered after its fingerprint was frozen, the Conductor must stop short — it does not produce any pass/fail result from the changed contract. (3) Mismatch surfaces to the Conductor, never the developer: the integrity stop is something the Conductor handles itself; the developer sees no HALT, prompt, pause, or choice during the build. (4) It is not a finding and not a stakes-class escalation: a mismatch does not raise a mid-flight escalation, does not enter the silent auto-fix loop, and is not recorded with any finding disposition (not fixed, dismissed, triaged-out, or escalated) — it is strictly a Conductor-owned integrity stop. (5) Per story: each story's verification compares that story's own contract against that story's own frozen fingerprint, and one story's drift does not bleed into another story's outcome.

How it was checked — and how strong that is. A QA agent checked the built workflow text against each of those five required behaviors, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for ways the wiring could fail; because this item carried high-risk findings it was re-checked after fixes were applied. Be clear about the strength of that verification: the deliverable is a markdown skill-instruction file (`workflow.md`), not executable code, and the live skill-invoke harness for this sprint is currently a skip — so nothing was actually run end-to-end. Verification was structured inspection of the workflow instructions against the frozen contract, not live execution. "Verified" here means a reviewer confirmed the instructions, if followed, would produce each required behavior — it does not mean the Conductor was driven through a drifted-contract scenario and watched. The contract's own self-check (run the story with an untouched contract, then again after altering it) was not executed against a running harness; it stands as the acceptance bar inspection was measured against.

Result. Verdict: PASS, recorded against the frozen contract (freeze fingerprint MATCH — the contract was not tampered with between assignment and verification). Six findings were raised: five fixed, one dismissed, none escalated, and a post-fix re-check came back clean (REFIXED). The findings are load-bearing and worth knowing: the adversarial reviewer caught that the freeze check had no invocation point — it was a defined step nobody called — and it was wired into the per-story flow; the `contract_integrity_stops` collection it writes to was given a real definition; and the re-check corrected *where* the gate fires. The build settled the freeze gate at story launch (the earliest sound point where the contract is read) rather than at a later verification boundary, on the reasoning that the contract cannot change between launch and verification, so one sha256 check at launch guards the same invariant — with an explicit instruction to future editors not to add a second check at the verification boundary. The check was merged via a trivial Conductor-resolved conflict. Net: PASS by inspection, with the substantive wiring gap found and fixed before merge — but understand this as reviewed-against-contract, not executed.

One caveat the reader should hold. The placement decision (launch-time vs. verify-time) is a defensible reading of the contract, not a literal one. The story and contract both describe the check as "the first step of that story's verification," and the build moved it earlier to launch with a written justification that the two points are equivalent. The logic is sound, but it is a design judgment the inspection accepted rather than a behavior the harness confirmed.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. This item adds the integrity backstop that keeps the single end-gate honest. Each story carries a verification contract, and when the Conductor assigns the story it records a frozen fingerprint of that contract (`frozen_sha256`). Before the Conductor lets a story be verified, it recomputes the sha256 of the contract file on disk and compares it to that frozen value. If they match, the contract is exactly what was agreed to and verification proceeds. If they differ, the contract drifted after it was frozen — so the Conductor refuses to produce a verification result from a moving target. The reason this matters: conduct collapses all the human touchpoints into one report at the end, and the developer trusts that report only if every per-story verification underneath it is sound. A contract that could silently change between freeze and verification would let a story "pass" against terms nobody approved, and the end-gate would inherit a corrupted result. The freeze check is the structural assertion that prevents that.

Why it is Conductor-facing and not a developer interruption. The deliberate design is that a mismatch surfaces *to the Conductor*, which owns and resolves it, and appears to the developer only as an informational note in the end-gate report — never as a mid-build HALT or prompt. This is the one sanctioned non-developer halt in the verification path. It is explicitly *not* a finding: it does not get a stakes class, does not enter the auto-fix loop, and carries no disposition (not fixed, dismissed, triaged-out, or escalated). To enforce that, the build keeps `contract_integrity_stops` as a dedicated collection, kept entirely separate from the finding/disposition machinery, so a mismatch can never be mistaken for a routine finding.

Governing decisions and spec section. The approach is driven by DEC-035 — adopt conduct, with one human gate at the end and no developer-facing HALT outside Phase 1 pre-flight; the decision's logic is that per-story verification soundness is what the single end-gate rests on, which is exactly what this check protects. The concrete behavior implements conduct spec §7, step 2 — assert `sha256(contract) == frozen_sha256` before per-story verification, and on mismatch surface to the Conductor rather than the developer. Per the story's Dev Notes, this is the *first* of the two narrow carve-outs from the DEC-035 #1 "no developer-facing HALT outside pre-flight" invariant: the Conductor-facing §7 freeze guard. DEC-036 — which adds a narrow, high-bar, developer-facing *mid-flight escalation tier* for stakes-class findings — was reviewed and deliberately leaves this item unchanged: a fingerprint mismatch is a structural integrity assertion about the Conductor's own inputs, not a stakes-class finding, so it gains no escalation behavior, no stakes classification, and no developer-facing pause.

What changed in the diff. All changes land in one file: `skills/momentum/skills/conductor/workflow.md`. The relevant additions are step 2.V (the contract-freeze check itself — compute live sha256, compare to frozen, branch on match/mismatch), its invocation hook at step 2.1.4 (the contract-freeze gate, fired at story launch as the earliest sound point), the `{{contract_integrity_stops}}` collection initialized in the step 2.0 state block, the integrity-stop guard that prevents a stopped story from proceeding to verifier dispatch (step 2.1.5), and the end-gate report section that renders any integrity stops to the developer as informational follow-up notes rather than as build-time interruptions.

▶ ③ The actual diff  (1 file changed, 87 insertions(+), 3 deletions(-))

Skip-redundant-QA routingconduct-coverage-disposition-branch  had a high-risk divergence → §03

This piece decides, for each work item the autonomous builder processes, whether that item gets its own quality-check run during the build or whether its check is deliberately postponed to a later, combined integration test that already covers it. The guarantee it gives is that postponing a check only changes when and where the check happens — never whether it happens — so without this piece a work item could be quietly waved through with no verification at all, or be checked twice and produce conflicting signals.

Delivered: The builder now reads each item's frozen "how should this be verified" setting and routes accordingly — running a dedicated check, or recording a skip that names the exact later test responsible for verifying it — and falls back to running a full check whenever that setting is missing, unrecognized, or names no downstream owner.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. This item is a routing fork in the autonomous build phase, so the contract was about *when and where a story gets verified* — never about how findings are judged. Seven required behaviors had to hold. First, a story frozen as `dedicated-run` must get its own QA verification run at build time, with no skip/defer marker on it. Second, a story frozen as `covered-by-composition` must *not* get a build-time run — and the build output must plainly say the run was skipped and name the specific integration scenario that will discharge it later at AVFL/merge (a silent skip, or a skip with no named owner, is a failure). Third, the Conductor must obey the *frozen* disposition even when the story's own text would suggest the opposite treatment — it reads the value, it never re-derives or overrides it. Fourth, a missing or garbage disposition must fall back to `dedicated-run` (verify, don't skip) rather than dropping coverage silently. Fifth and most important, deferring to composition must change only timing and venue: a stakes-class finding (auth-isolation, irreversible/destructive, high-blast-radius/architecture) that surfaces when the deferred scenario runs downstream must still be routed out of the silent auto-fix path and rendered in the report — this branch must neither perform nor suppress that routing. Sixth, no duplicate or widened verification: a composed story is verified exactly once, downstream, never both at build and at merge.

How it was checked — and how strong that is. A QA agent checked the built workflow text against each of those required behaviors, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for ways the branch could leak (a silent skip, an override of the frozen value, a finding getting demoted by the deferral). Because this item is a markdown skill-instruction file — the Conductor's `workflow.md` — and its harness profile is `skill-invoke` with no live runtime, the verification was structured inspection against the frozen contract, not live execution. No build was actually run over real stories; reviewers traced the workflow's branch logic, guards, and recorded outputs and confirmed they match what each scenario demands. Read "verified" here as "the instruction provably specifies the required behavior," not "the behavior was observed running." That distinction matters most for the stakes-class boundary (scenario 5) and the no-duplicate-run guarantee (scenario 6), whose downstream half — the integration scenario actually discharging at AVFL/merge — is explicitly *not yet wired* in the Conductor (the workflow flags this as an intentional incremental gap owned by a later story); what was verified is that the build phase correctly records the deferral and hands off, not that the discharge loop closes end to end.

Result. PASS, on round 5, with the frozen contract matching (no scope drift). Four findings were raised and all four fixed; none escalated, none dismissed. The most consequential catch came from the post-fix recheck: the adversarial pass found that a story marked `covered-by-composition` but with a *null/missing* `covered_by_scenario` would have skipped its build-time run with no named downstream owner — a silent coverage gap. That was fixed by adding an explicit guard (a dedicated "Path B guard" check) that falls back to `dedicated-run` whenever the scenario name is absent, mirroring the missing-disposition safe default and making the naming requirement a hard precondition for skipping. The recheck verdict was REFIXED. One incidental note: the agent had edited the story file itself, which the Conductor reverted; the change was then merged via semantic conflict resolution that preserved the surrounding build-phase steps.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. The build phase needed a first-class way to say "don't verify this story in isolation at build time — a composed integration scenario will cover it later." Without it, the flow had only two bad options: run every story's own QA pass at build (redundant and noisy for work that's meant to be verified together) or drop the coverage silently. The chosen design is a deliberately *thin, mechanical* fork placed inside the per-story build-phase loop (new step 2.C). For each story the Conductor reads the frozen `coverage_disposition`: `dedicated-run` routes to a normal build-time QA run; `covered-by-composition` skips that run and instead writes a `coverage-disposition-deferred` record that states the run was skipped and names the integration scenario owning the downstream discharge. The branch is intentionally not allowed to think — it reads the value set upstream at planning/contract-freeze and acts on it; it never computes, infers, or overrides the disposition. The one judgment-shaped behavior is the conservative safe default: missing, unrecognized, or (after the fix) un-named dispositions fall back to `dedicated-run` so coverage is never silently lost.

Why it's bounded this way — the load-bearing boundary. The single most important design constraint is that this branch decides *WHEN and WHERE* verification runs, never *HOW* findings are classified. The workflow carries an explicit "TIMING-AND-VENUE ONLY" note and a "STAKES-CLASS BOUNDARY" note asserting that choosing `covered-by-composition` only moves the run's timing/venue — it can never demote, hide, silence, or auto-resolve any finding, including a stakes-class one. A stakes-class finding that surfaces when the deferred scenario runs at AVFL/merge is still routed out of the silent auto-fix path and rendered in the report *by the finding schema and report, not by this branch*. This separation is what keeps a planner's "verify it in composition" intent from ever becoming a backdoor around stakes handling.

Governing decisions. This item realizes DEC-035 (adopt conduct; one human gate at the end; legible auto-fix loop) by making the deferral explicit and auditable rather than an implicit gap. DEC-036 is cited deliberately to assert what it does *not* do: DEC-036 narrowly amends DEC-035 to add a stakes-gated mid-flight escalation tier, legible finding dispositions, and an anti-rubber-stamp end-gate — all of which concern *how findings are classified and surfaced*, which is exactly the dimension this coverage branch is forbidden to touch. The story's Dev Notes and References make this explicit, and the built workflow records the boundary in-line so a future reader cannot mistake `covered-by-composition` for a way to bypass stakes routing. The governing conduct spec section 7 (the `coverage_disposition` / `covered-by-composition` definition and the AVFL/merge discharge by a named integration scenario, spec §7 step 4) is the source the branch implements; the downstream discharge point is spec §5 / Phase 3 step 4, which this story records a handoff to but does not itself wire.

What changed in the diff. One file: `skills/momentum/skills/conductor/workflow.md`. The substantive addition is the new step 2.C (the coverage-disposition branch) plus its invocation hook in step 2.1.5, which routes per-story before verifier dispatch. Inside 2.C you'll see the safe-default check for missing/unrecognized dispositions, Path A (`dedicated-run`), the Path B guard for a null/empty `covered_by_scenario` (the fix from the recheck), Path B (`covered-by-composition`) with its deferral record, and the no-second-run and stakes-class boundary guardrails.

Can-we-even-start guardsconduct-preflight-halts  had a high-risk divergence → §03

This story is the front door of every conduct build: before any work starts, the system runs a handful of "can we even begin?" checks (no sprint, sprint not turned on, missing approvals, leftover broken state) and then quietly cleans up the workspace from any prior interrupted run so the build starts from a known-good state. The guarantee it gives is that a build only ever starts from a validated, clean baseline, and once running it never stops to pester the developer — with one narrow, deliberate exception for genuinely dangerous moments. Without this piece, a build could either start on top of broken leftover state or constantly interrupt the developer, defeating the whole point of an unattended build.

Delivered: The conduct system now has a working start-up phase that refuses to begin when something is genuinely wrong, automatically resets and re-runs any story left half-finished by a prior interrupted session without asking the developer anything, and then hands off to the build from a clean baseline.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. This item defines Phase 1 of the Conductor — the pre-flight stage that runs before any build work begins. The frozen contract required five behaviors. First, five "cannot-start" guards (H1 no active sprint, H2 sprint never activated, H3 missing approvals, H4/H5 stalled or inconsistent state) must each, on its own, stop the Conductor before any story is dispatched and before the working tree is touched — and the stop must name *which* guard fired and why. Second, once the build has actually started, those guards must never be re-asked and no new cannot-start guard may appear. Third, the git reconcile-on-start must run silently: no resume-vs-cleanup prompt, no asking the developer to choose how to handle leftover state. Fourth, a story left "in-progress" by a prior interrupted run must be automatically reset to a clean, dispatchable state and re-dispatched, with the build then starting from a known-good tree. Fifth, the Conductor's own written invariant must state that no developer-facing HALT exists outside pre-flight *except* a narrow mid-flight escalation tier, and must name both carve-outs as distinct — the Conductor-facing freeze guard (internal, the developer never sees it) and the developer-facing mid-flight tier — while confining that developer-facing tier strictly to irreversible-and-imminent or build-invalidating triggers (routine and even merely high-blast-radius findings must not pause the build).

How it was checked, and how strong that is. A QA agent verified the built file against each required behavior, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for ways the guards or the invariant could be wrong or under-specified; this item was flagged high-risk and re-checked after its fixes landed. You should read "verified" here narrowly. The deliverable is a markdown skill-instruction file (`workflow.md`), and the verification method for this contract is `skill-invoke` against a harness that is effectively non-executing for these files — so the checking was done by structured inspection of the workflow text against the frozen contract, not by running a live Conductor through the four pre-flight conditions. The sibling scaffold story in the same build records its evidence identically ("satisfied by structural reading"). In plain terms: a careful, adversarial reading confirmed the workflow *says and structures* the right behavior; no automated run *observed* the Conductor actually halting on a missing sprint or silently re-dispatching a stale story. The contract's own "how the dev self-checks" section asks for exactly that observe-don't-reason live invocation — that step was not executed by the harness and is worth doing by hand if you want execution-level assurance.

Result. Verdict: PASS, with freeze status MATCH (the build was checked against the contract that was frozen, unchanged). Five findings were raised and all five fixed: a backward story-transition needing `--force`, the H3 approvals field, the H2 activate command, an H4 dependency check, and — the significant one — a major H5 finding where the workflow's status list disagreed with the canonical story state machine, which was re-checked and refixed. The reviewer also caught that the Conductor had reverted a story-file edit. Zero findings were escalated or dismissed. The item is merged.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. Momentum's old sprint-dev loop interrupted the developer constantly — a resume/cleanup prompt on every restart plus a stream of mid-build questions — so you could never just walk away while a build ran. This item collapses all of that to a single decision point at the start. Phase 1 front-loads every developer-facing "you can't start because…" check into five guards that fire *before* any story is dispatched, so a bad starting condition is caught early and named clearly rather than discovered mid-build. Then the git reconcile-on-start is made deliberately silent and decisive: instead of asking "resume or clean up?", it auto-resets any stale `in-progress` story from a crashed prior run back to `ready-for-dev` and re-dispatches it, reaching a known-good tree on its own. The design choice is that pre-flight is the *only* gate the developer answers on the routine path; after the one go-ahead, the build runs unattended to a single end-gate.

Why it's built this way — the governing decisions. This implements DEC-035 (adopt conduct: one human gate at the end, no developer-facing HALT outside pre-flight, a legible anti-firehose auto-fix loop) — specifically DEC-035 binding decision #1, the invariant that there is no developer-facing HALT outside Phase 1. That invariant is amended twice, and the subtle work of this item is encoding *both* amendments without conflating them. The conduct breakdown softened it once for the Conductor-facing section-7 freeze guard — an internal guard the developer never sees. DEC-036 softens it a second time, and this carve-out *is* developer-facing: a narrow, high-bar, stakes-gated mid-flight escalation tier that can pause the build only for irreversible-and-imminent or build-invalidating findings. So the workflow's invariant statement names two distinct exceptions — (a) Conductor-facing freeze guard, (b) developer-facing mid-flight tier — and pins the developer-facing tier to that narrow bar, with everything else (routine, even high-blast-radius) staying on the silent auto-fix path or deferred to the end-gate. The story's Dev Notes cite conduct spec section 6 (non-interactive reconcile-on-start), section 8 (git-state handling the reconcile draws on), and section 7 (the freeze guard, referenced but not implemented here).

What changed in the diff. A single file: `skills/momentum/skills/conductor/workflow.md`, where the new Phase 1 pre-flight section was added — the five H1–H5 cannot-start guards with their stop messages, the non-interactive reconcile-on-start procedure with its known-good end condition, the re-softened two-carve-out invariant, and the closing no-other-HALT statement. The diff below is that workflow text.

The code-review tooling

The non-interactive review drivercode-review-adapter-noninteractive-driver

This story builds the thin connector that lets conduct run its automated code reviewer on a single story's changes completely hands-off — it forces the reviewer to run start-to-finish, report what it finds, and never stop to ask a person a question or edit any files itself. Without it, the reviewer would freeze the whole autonomous build the first time it hit its usual "pause and confirm" prompt, or worse, start rewriting files behind the builder's back.

Delivered: conduct can now run adversarial code review on one story's changes unattended — it returns the full list of findings (or a clear "nothing found"), touches no files, and stops early with a plain, fix-it message if the reviewer's required config is missing instead of failing somewhere deep inside.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. The frozen contract pinned this adapter to six observable behaviors, all flowing from one idea: the existing adversarial code-review tool was built for a human sitting at the keyboard, and this adapter had to make it safe to call with nobody watching. First, an unattended run had to go from start to finish without ever stopping to ask a question — no prompt, no confirmation, no "press enter to continue" — and still hand back the review's findings, including a deliberately planted issue. Second, it had to be strictly look-don't-touch: capture the working tree before, run, capture after, and the two states must be byte-for-byte identical (the adapter reports, it never edits or fixes). Third, every HALT or pause the underlying tool would normally raise in a human session had to be suppressed or auto-answered so the run never blocks. Fourth, a clean diff had to come back as an explicit "nothing found" answer, not an error or an ambiguous empty response. Fifth and sixth, the adapter had to check up front that the review tool's required config file is present, and if it's missing, stop early with a clear, actionable error that names the missing file and says what to provide — rather than failing opaquely somewhere deep inside the tool. A seventh behavior bounded the review to a single story's diff per call, and an eighth confirmed the adapter does transport only: it must not classify findings by stakes, assign dispositions, escalate, or fix anything.

How it was checked. A QA agent verified the built skill against each of those required behaviors, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for bugs; because this story carried real risk (a wrong config check or a leaked HALT would stall the whole build phase), it went through fix-and-recheck before passing. You should read "verified" here precisely: the deliverables are markdown instruction files — the code-reviewer skill's `SKILL.md` and `workflow.md` — and the runtime harness for this sprint is set to "skip." So verification was done by structured inspection of the instructions against the frozen contract, not by live execution of the adapter against a real diff. Nobody actually ran the adapter end-to-end, watched a planted bug come back, or diffed a working tree before and after. What was checked is that the written protocol, read carefully, demands each required behavior — the explicit HALT-suppression rules, the read-only invariant, the no-findings branch, and the pre-run config check are all present and internally consistent. Treat this as a strong instruction review, not a passing test run.

Result. The verdict is PASS, with the frozen contract recorded as a MATCH (the built item was checked against the same contract that was frozen, not a moved goalpost). The review surfaced five findings and all five were fixed, and the post-fix recheck came back CLEAN. The most consequential was a genuine bug: the pre-run config check originally looked for the config file in the wrong place. Because conduct runs each story in a git worktree, and git worktrees do not materialize untracked/gitignored files, checking the current directory would have falsely reported the config as missing on every run. The fix points the check at the main checkout via `git rev-parse --git-common-dir` so it inspects where `_bmad/bmm/config.yaml` actually lives. The other fixes hardened the contract's edges: a gap where a class of findings could be silently dropped (AC4), incomplete HALT-suppression coverage (AC3), and a corrected config schema. The skill was merged.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. Rather than building a second reviewer, this story wraps the existing adversarial code-review engine in a thin adapter — a transport layer that constrains *how* the tool is invoked without reimplementing any review logic. The reason is that the existing tool is good at finding bugs but was designed for an interactive, human-in-the-loop session: it can pause and HALT for confirmation, and it can be told to apply fixes. Both are incompatible with conduct, where the Conductor builds and reviews every story autonomously with no mid-stream prompts. An interactive HALT would freeze the build phase the moment any story is reviewed, and the tool's fix-application mode would mutate the working tree out from under the Conductor — which owns all git mutation. The adapter neutralizes both hazards with a single non-interactive, report-only entry point: it suppresses the tool's entire activation ceremony and every HALT (it auto-resolves from the inputs already supplied), it runs strictly read-only so the tree is never touched, and it returns findings in full (including an explicit no-findings result on a clean diff). It was also kept deliberately narrow — pure transport. It carries findings; it does not classify them by stakes, assign dispositions, escalate, or fix. That keeps it the lowest-effort piece that unblocks the per-story review leg without entangling it with the escalation machinery, which lives in separate stories that consume this adapter's output. A small but real piece of robustness lives here too: the adapter checks for the review tool's required config (`_bmad/bmm/config.yaml`) before running, so a setup gap surfaces as a named, actionable error instead of an opaque failure deep inside the tool.

Governing decisions. This adapter is the non-interactive transport that lets the per-story review leg run inside the single-end-gate flow mandated by DEC-035 (adopt conduct; one human end-gate at the close of the flow; the auto-fix loop must stay legible). The story is explicitly UNCHANGED by DEC-036 — the amendment that adds narrow, stakes-gated mid-flight escalation — precisely because this adapter is pure transport: stakes classification, the dispositions of fixed / dismissed / triaged-out / escalated, and the mid-flight-versus-end-gate timing tiers are all out of scope and owned by downstream stories. (Note for the reader: the diff you see below reflects later sibling stories layered on top — `code-review-adapter-normalize-triage` added schema normalization and `stakes_class` population, and `code-review-adapter-retire-stub` removed the old in-house reviewer body. This story's specific contribution is the non-interactive driver, the report-only invariants, and the pre-run config check.) On the conduct spec itself, this work implements section 4 (the tool decision — the adversarial review tool is the chosen engine for the per-story review leg) and section 10 (the gap map — it closes the config-scaffolding gap), and it answers spec open question 3 (what the required config scaffolding is and how the adapter surfaces an actionable error when it is absent).

Files to look at in the diff. The change is entirely in the code-reviewer skill: `skills/momentum/skills/code-reviewer/SKILL.md` (the skill's identity and boundaries — what the adapter is and, pointedly, what it is not) and `skills/momentum/skills/code-reviewer/workflow.md` (the binding execution protocol — the read-only and never-pause invariants up top, the step-1 config-presence check that resolves the main worktree root, the diff-scoping and no-findings handling, and the suppression of the underlying tool's activation and HALT flow).

▶ ③ The actual diff  (2 files changed, 288 insertions(+), 3 deletions(-))

Translating review output into the rulebookcode-review-adapter-normalize-triage  had a high-risk divergence → §03

This piece is the translator that takes the output of the code reviewer and reshapes it into the one common "finding" format that conduct's auto-fixer understands, tagging each finding with how high the stakes are so the system knows which problems can be fixed silently and which deserve a human's attention. Without it, the fixer would either be locked to one specific review tool's private vocabulary or would receive findings with no stakes signal at all, which would quietly defeat the whole safety mechanism that decides when to escalate to a person.

Delivered: conduct now has a working adapter that converts the code reviewer's tool-specific result buckets into the system's single canonical finding format, drops only the reviewer's own noise, and stamps every surviving finding with a stakes class drawn from the shared rubric.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. This item is the adapter that takes the adversarial code reviewer's output and reshapes it so the rest of the autonomous build can consume it. The frozen contract demanded five concrete behaviors. First, the three review buckets that represent real findings — "decision-needed," "patch," and "defer" — must all survive and come out the other side in the build's single, uniform finding shape, each stamped as originating from the bmad code-review source; none of the three may be silently dropped. Second, the reviewer's own noise bucket ("dismiss" — false positives and things handled elsewhere) must be thrown away here and never appear downstream, while legitimate findings from the same run still make it through. Third, every finding that flows forward must carry a populated stakes-class value — never blank — and when the reviewer gives no security or stakes hint, the value must default to "routine." Fourth, when a finding genuinely is a stakes case (a security/auth issue, a destructive/irreversible operation, a high-blast-radius change), it must be tagged with that class — and crucially, the tag must come from the project's shared stakes rubric rather than being guessed or hard-coded inside this adapter. Fifth, the instruction text itself must spell out, in plain words, that the reviewer's dropped "dismiss" noise is a *different thing* from the downstream fixer's "dismissed-with-rationale" outcome, and must forbid the adapter from pre-filtering legitimate findings on its own judgment.

How it was checked — and how strong that check is. A QA agent checked the built item against each of those required behaviors, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for bugs; because this item drew high-risk findings, it was re-checked after the fixes landed. You should read "verified" here precisely: the deliverable is a markdown skill instruction file (plus its SKILL.md command surface), and the skill-invoke runtime harness for this sprint was not run — every build record in this sprint carries no harness and no runtime execution. So the contract's scenarios were confirmed by structured inspection of the instruction text against the frozen contract, not by executing the adapter over live sample change sets. The reviewers read the workflow steps and confirmed the bucket-to-schema mapping, the source stamping, the rubric consultation, the no-signal default, and the two-dismiss distinction are all written into the instructions as specified — they did not run a real code review through it and observe the emitted findings. That is the honest ceiling on this evidence: the *instructions* provably say the right thing; the *behavior* was not exercised end-to-end.

Result. The verdict is PASS, with the build result recording a freeze MATCH (the item satisfied the frozen contract it was committed to). The check was not clean on the first pass — the adversarial review surfaced real issues that were fixed and then re-verified (recorded as REFIXED): the canonical "verdict" field had been overloaded with the bmad bucket label and was separated out; the "defer" bucket's legitimate-finding flag was corrected; and a stale "PURE TRANSPORT" identity claim in the workflow's critical block was replaced. Of five findings on this item, four were fixed and one (a model-pin) was dismissed with rationale. The delivered files are `skills/momentum/skills/code-reviewer/workflow.md` and `skills/momentum/skills/code-reviewer/SKILL.md`.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. conduct (the in-session, autonomous rewrite of the build loop) feeds its auto-fix loop from several finding sources, and its fixer understands exactly one finding format. The adversarial reviewer it uses, bmad-code-review, does not speak that format — it sorts results into its own tool-specific triage buckets (decision-needed / patch / defer / dismiss) and emits no stakes signal at all. Rather than teach the fixer the reviewer's vocabulary (which would weld the fixer to one specific review tool) or drop the stakes signal entirely (which would collapse the escalation tier), this item builds a thin adapter that sits between them: it maps the reviewer's legitimate buckets into the canonical finding schema, stamps `source=bmad-code-review` on each, and consumes the project's shared stakes rubric to attach a `stakes_class`. The ownership boundary is deliberately tight — this adapter only does the *wiring*. It does not own or redefine the canonical schema (that belongs to the `directed-fix-finding-schema` item it conforms to), and it does not own or fork the rubric's classification logic (that belongs to the `stakes-classification-rubric` item it merely calls). That separation is why the adapter can stay small and why a future change to the rubric or schema does not require touching it.

Why it's built this way — the governing decisions. The approach is driven by DEC-035 and DEC-036. DEC-035 established conduct's legible auto-fix loop feeding a single human end-gate; this finding stream is what that loop consumes. The two binding amendments come from DEC-036. Amendment D2 is why the adapter populates `stakes_class` at all: stakes class is the routing key DEC-036 uses to decide whether a finding stays on the always-silent routine auto-fix path or qualifies for the narrow mid-flight escalation tier — so D2 requires the field to be populated via the shared rubric, defaulting to `routine` (or null) when there is no signal. If the adapter emitted findings without a stakes class, that routing could not run and DEC-036's escalation tier would collapse back into DEC-035's absolutist silent-auto-fix model. Amendment D3 (and its clarification) is why the adapter drops only the reviewer's own `dismiss` noise bucket and nothing more: D3 makes the reviewer's noise filter a *distinct concept* from the conduct fixer's `dismissed` disposition (a legitimate finding the fixer waves off with a required rationale and renders in the report). The instruction text is written to forbid the adapter from pre-emptively suppressing any decision-needed / patch / defer finding on its own judgment, because doing so would rob the fixer of findings it is supposed to see-and-dismiss-with-rationale and would silently shrink the end-gate report's dismissal ledger.

What changed in the diff below. The work is two skill-instruction files. The primary deliverable is `skills/momentum/skills/code-reviewer/workflow.md`, which now drives bmad-code-review non-interactively, drops the noise bucket, normalizes the three legitimate buckets into the canonical schema, stamps the source, and consults the rubric to set `stakes_class` (with the explicit two-dismiss warning and the rubric no-fork guard written in). The companion `skills/momentum/skills/code-reviewer/SKILL.md` was repointed so the code-reviewer command activates this adapter rather than its retired stub body.
