Retiring the placeholder reviewercode-review-adapter-retire-stub

This story's job was to make sure there is exactly one real code reviewer in the build, by retiring an old placeholder reviewer and pointing the review command at the genuine review engine. The guarantee it gives conduct is that every time the build asks for a code review, it gets actual review findings in the shape the rest of the system understands — never a hollow placeholder. Without this, the build could route to the dead stub, believe a change had been reviewed when it had only received empty placeholder text, and ship unexamined code.

Delivered: Invoking the code-review command now always drives the real adversarial review engine and returns properly shaped findings, while the old placeholder reviewer is gone and can no longer be reached.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. The frozen contract treated this as a black-box behavior change with five required outcomes. First, invoking the code-reviewer command on a small change containing a genuine issue had to return *real* review findings in the normalized shape — each finding able to carry a disposition (fixed, dismissed, triaged-out, or escalated) and a stakes class (security/auth-isolation; irreversible/destructive; high-blast-radius/architecture; or default routine) — and never the old placeholder stub text. Second, invoking the legacy in-house stub reviewer directly had to produce *no* review findings — its placeholder body had to be gone. Third, no path through the command could reach the stub: the command always drives the adapter. Fourth, repeating the command on identical input had to route to the adapter both times with a consistent output shape and no placeholder fallback. Fifth — the scope guard — the change had to add *no* new escalation behavior of its own: no mid-flight escalation tier, no newly-invented stakes classification, no change to how dismissals are rendered, and no change to the end-gate. The only thing this story was allowed to change was *where* the findings come from.

How it was checked. A QA agent verified the built item against each of those required behaviors, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for bugs and overreach; because this item carries deletion risk, it was re-checked after fixes (the recorded recheck came back CLEAN). Be clear-eyed about how strong that verification is: these are markdown skill and command files — the runtime harness for this sprint is set to skip, so nothing was *executed* live. Verification was by structured inspection against the frozen contract, not live invocation. The reviewers read the retired stub skill, the repointed command shim, and the supporting adapter wiring and reasoned about whether the contract's behaviors hold — they did not actually run the command on a staged diff and observe a finding stream come back. So "verified" here means "inspected and judged to satisfy the contract," not "ran and watched it pass." What the inspection confirms is concrete and checkable on the page: the skill body now states the in-house reviewer is retired and contains no review logic ("Invoking this skill without following workflow.md produces no review findings"), and the command shim ends with an explicit "The in-house stub reviewer is retired. No invocation path through this command reaches stub output." The scope guard is also visible in the text — the skill explicitly disclaims being a fixer, escalator, or disposition assigner.

Result. The recorded verdict is PASS, with the build's output matching the frozen contract (freeze: MATCH). Three findings were raised during review: one was fixed (a touches-path correction) and two were dismissed with rationale (one challenging the story's premise on the grounds the adapter was effectively already converted, and one about a bmad cross-reference); zero were escalated. One incidental edit to the story file itself was reverted to keep the change to deliverables only. The reviewers classified the core deletion as Gate-A reversible (a deletion that can be undone, warranting no escalation). The item was merged at 2026-06-04T20:27:47Z.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. Before this change, two things both claimed to be "the reviewer": the in-house code-reviewer stub, which only ever emitted placeholder output (not genuine adversarial findings), and the new adapter that wraps the real `bmad-code-review` engine. Two predecessor stories in this sprint had already stood the adapter up — one made it run non-interactively so the Conductor can drive it autonomously, and one normalized its triage output into conduct's canonical finding shape (dispositions plus stakes classes). With those in place, the stub was dead weight and an active trap: if the command ever routed to it, the build phase would believe code had been reviewed when it had only received placeholder text. This story closes that trap by collapsing to a single reviewer of record. The stub's placeholder reviewer body was deleted — the skill now explicitly carries no review logic and delegates everything to the engine via its `workflow.md` — and the command shim was repointed to drive the adapter end to end. It is deliberately a pure plumbing change: deletion plus a shim repoint, adding no new normalization or classification of its own. That minimalism is the point — the findings flowing downstream were already in the consumable normalized shape because the predecessors produced them, so this story only changes the *origin* of the stream, never its contents.

Governing decisions. This work serves DEC-035 (adopt conduct with a single human end-gate, a report organized by user-facing functionality, and a legible auto-fix loop) by guaranteeing the auto-fix loop and end-gate are fed real adapter findings rather than placeholder non-findings — legibility is meaningless if the findings are fake. Critically, the story is explicitly UNCHANGED by DEC-036. DEC-036 amends DEC-035 to permit a narrow, high-bar, stakes-gated mid-flight escalation tier, introduces the `escalated` disposition, requires the report to render dismissals with rationale, and adds an anti-rubber-stamp end-gate — and *none* of that machinery is created or touched here. The only DEC-036-relevant guarantee this story carries is provenance: the escalation, dismissal-rendering, and end-gate components downstream now receive their findings from the adapter rather than the retired stub. The governing spec sections cited by the story are section 4 ("Retire the stub") — the deletion and the single-reviewer-of-record rationale — and section 12 — the command repoint onto the adapter.

What changed in the diff below. Two files. `skills/momentum/skills/code-reviewer/SKILL.md` — the in-house stub's placeholder reviewer body is removed and replaced with a shim that declares the stub retired and delegates all review execution to the `bmad-code-review` engine via `workflow.md`. `skills/momentum/commands/code-reviewer.md` — the command shim, repointed to invoke the adapter and stating plainly that no invocation path reaches the stub. (The build also added EDD eval scaffolding under the code-reviewer skill's `evals/` directory as supporting verification material.)

Pointing sprint-dev at the real reviewercode-review-adapter-repoint-sprint-dev  had a high-risk divergence → §03

This story rewires the sprint-dev workflow's per-story code review so it goes through conduct's single shared review adapter instead of a retired, dead review path — guaranteeing that every story's changes are reviewed by the same reviewer, producing findings in the one consistent shape the rest of conduct (triage, auto-fix, escalation, the human end-gate) is built to read. Without this piece, sprint-dev would either call a reviewer that no longer exists (breaking the run) or emit findings in a stale, incompatible shape that downstream stages can't process — quietly splitting the practice into two divergent review paths.

Delivered: Sprint-dev now sends each story's real code changes to the shared review adapter and gets back normalized findings, with no remaining reference to the old retired reviewer.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. The frozen contract for this item was entirely about *where the sprint-dev per-story review step goes and what it hands back* — nothing about new behavior. Five things had to hold. First, when a sprint-dev run reaches its per-story code-review step, that step must actually reach the shared bmad-code-review adapter and produce genuine review findings — not the placeholder output of the old retired stub, and not an error or a skipped step. Second, the findings it returns must be in the adapter's *normalized* shape: structurally identical to what you'd get by invoking the adapter directly on the same change. Third, that must hold everywhere review happens in the flow — no stub-style output should survive anywhere. Fourth, the surrounding review flow must be untouched: review still triggers at the same point, reviews the same change, and feeds its result into the same next step — the *only* observable difference being that the output is now adapter-normalized. Fifth, because this is pure wiring, the step must add no new behavior of its own — no mid-flight escalation, no disposition handling (fixed / dismissed / triaged-out / escalated), and no end-gate or report rendering. In plain terms: swap the callee, change nothing else.

How it was checked, and how strong that check is. A QA agent verified the built file against each of those required behaviors, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for bugs; the high-risk finding was re-checked after the fix. You should read "verified" carefully here, because the honest strength is bounded: the deliverable is a single markdown workflow file (`skills/momentum/skills/sprint-dev/workflow.md`), the change type is `skill-instruction`, and the runtime harness for this sprint is in "skip." So this was structured inspection against the frozen contract — reading the repointed call sites and the diff-generation logic to confirm they satisfy the required behaviors — not a live sprint-dev run that actually executed the adapter and compared output shapes. No real sprint was driven through Phase 4b to observe normalized findings come back. Treat the PASS as "the instructions provably say the right thing on inspection," not "we watched it run."

Result. The recorded verdict is PASS, with the freeze check reading MATCH (the built item matches the contract that was frozen at planning). Six findings surfaced during review: five were fixed and one was dismissed, with zero escalated. The load-bearing finding was a HIGH bug in how the per-story diff range is computed — the original approach diffed against the branch tip using a tip-relative `~N` offset, which is wrong for sequential merges (and a three-dot diff against the post-merge branch returns empty under rebase-then-fast-forward). The fix bounced twice (producing an empty diff) before converging on a `pre_merge_sha`-based merge-boundary range, which the re-check marks as REFIXED. That fix is what you see at Phase 4b lines 414-424 of the workflow: a two-dot `{{pre_merge_sha[slug]}}..story/{slug}` primary range with a `{{pre_merge_sha[slug]}}..{{post_merge_sha[slug]}}` fallback for when the story branch is already gone. The item is recorded as merged.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. The whole point of the conduct rewrite is that code review has exactly *one* entry point across the practice: the bmad-code-review adapter, which drives the underlying bmad-code-review non-interactively and normalizes every finding into one consistent shape (each finding stamped with `source="bmad-code-review"`, `stakes_class`, severity, file, and description). Everything downstream — the consolidated fix queue, targeted fixes, selective re-review, and the human end-gate report — depends on that single normalized shape. The sprint-dev workflow, however, still wired its per-story review step to the legacy `momentum:code-reviewer` stub. This story repoints those call sites onto the adapter so sprint-dev emits the same normalized findings the rest of the practice consumes. The alternative — leaving two review paths alive — would mean two findings shapes, two places to maintain, and silent drift between what conduct sees and what sprint-dev sees; and once the stub is retired (its sibling story), the surviving sprint-dev caller would simply break. Critically, this was kept to *call-site wiring only*: the trigger point, the input handed to review, and how the result feeds the consolidated fix queue all stay the same — only the callee changed.

Governing decisions and spec sections. This is driven by DEC-035, which adopts conduct and establishes the single-adapter / single-human-end-gate direction that motivates one code-review entry point. The story's References and Dev Notes are explicit that it is unchanged by DEC-036: DEC-036 amends DEC-035 to permit a narrow, stakes-gated mid-flight escalation tier and to require legible dispositions plus an anti-rubber-stamp end-gate — but none of that touches how a *caller* reaches the adapter, so this story deliberately inherits no escalation, disposition, or timing-tier behavior. The exemption was considered and recorded intentionally. The governing conduct-spec sections cited by the authoring brief are Section 4 ("replace every momentum:code-reviewer invocation" — the mandate this story executes for sprint-dev specifically) and Section 12 (call-site wiring scope for repointing callers onto the adapter).

What changed in the diff below. A single file: `skills/momentum/skills/sprint-dev/workflow.md`. The substantive edits are in Phase 4b ("Per-Story Code Review," step 4.1), where each review call now spawns `momentum:code-reviewer` as the bmad-code-review adapter (the adapter wrapper, not the retired stub), and in Phase 4d's selective re-review, which routes `source="bmad-code-review"` fix items back through the same adapter. Folded into the same change is the diff-range fix described in the verification section — the `pre_merge_sha`-based two-dot range that determines exactly what each story's review actually looks at. That fix is wiring-adjacent (it governs the *input* the adapter receives) rather than new conduct behavior, so it stays inside this story's scope.

Pointing quick-fix at the real reviewercode-review-adapter-repoint-quick-fix  had a high-risk divergence → §03

This story rewires the "quick-fix" flow — the fastest single-fix path a developer reaches for — so its code-review step calls the current, canonical review engine instead of an old retired one. The guarantee it gives conduct is that even the speedy single-fix path gets the same real, normalized code review every other path gets; without it, quick-fix would point at a review that no longer exists, and could appear to pass while no review actually happened.

Delivered: The quick-fix flow now runs its code review through the live review engine and returns the same normalized findings as every other Momentum review, with no leftover reference to the old retired path.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. The frozen contract for this item set a single bar: when the quick-fix flow reaches its code-review step, that step must call the new bmad-code-review adapter and nothing else. Seven concrete behaviors had to hold. First, the review step must run through the adapter and come back with adapter-normalized findings — the exact same shape you'd get calling the adapter directly — not the old stub's raw placeholder output. Second, no trace of the retired stub could remain anywhere in the quick-fix flow text: no call, no fallback, no leftover comment. The remaining behaviors are all "pass-through" obligations — the quick-fix caller must inherit the adapter's behavior without changing it: routine findings stay on the always-auto-fixed path with no quick-fix-introduced gate or prompt (DEC-036); dismissed findings stay visible together with their required non-empty rationale rather than being silently dropped; escalated findings (raised, not silently fixed) get surfaced to the developer rather than swallowed or auto-resolved, and quick-fix must not widen the narrow escalation criteria on its own. The final requirement is the umbrella check: a quick-fix review and a direct adapter run on the same change must be semantically equivalent — same finding shape, stakes-class labels, and dispositions — with the only difference being the call site.

How it was checked. A QA agent verified the built change against each of those required behaviors, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for bugs the QA pass might have missed; because the adversarial pass surfaced a high-severity issue, the item was re-checked after the fix (the recorded recheck came back clean). Be clear about what "verified" means here, though: the artifact is a markdown skill workflow file, and the skill-invoke runtime harness for this sprint is not actually executing flows — it is in skip mode. So the contract's scenarios, which are written as "run the quick-fix flow to its review step and observe the output," were not run live. Verification was done by structured inspection of the changed instruction text against the frozen contract — reading the workflow to confirm it now calls the adapter, that the stub reference is gone, and that no caller-side gate, filter, suppression, or escalation-widening was introduced around the adapter's findings. That is a credible way to verify a pure call-site repoint, but it is inspection, not a recorded end-to-end execution, and "verified" should be read in that light.

Result. The verdict was PASS, with the change merged. The contract froze a MATCH (the built item was checked against the same contract that was frozen at planning time). Four findings were raised during review and all four were fixed; none were escalated and none dismissed, and the post-fix recheck was clean. The most load-bearing finding was a HIGH-severity catch during review — the review diff range had been scoped too narrowly (only the last commit) and a stale sibling eval file was flagged; both were corrected, and a stray path-filter was removed. The recorded evidence is the two deliverables touched: the quick-fix workflow itself (`skills/momentum/skills/quick-fix/workflow.md`) and its companion eval doc (`skills/momentum/skills/quick-fix/evals/eval-quickfix-code-review.md`).

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. This story is deliberately the smallest possible change: it only repoints which thing the quick-fix code-review step calls. A prior story retired the old code-review stub and stood up the bmad-code-review adapter as the single canonical entry point for code review across the whole practice. Once the stub was gone, any caller still wired to it became a dangling reference — and quick-fix is exactly the flow developers reach for when they want the fastest turnaround, so a silent break here is especially costly. The worst failure mode isn't a loud crash; it's a review that reports "passed" when it never actually ran. The fix is to wire quick-fix to the adapter so a single-story fix gets the same normalized findings every other Momentum review consumer already receives. Critically, this is wiring and not review semantics: the review engine, the finding shape, the stakes-class taxonomy, the dispositions, and the escalation tiers all live upstream in the adapter and the Conductor flow. Quick-fix is intended to be a thin pass-through that inherits all of that for free — which is why the contract is written almost entirely as "do not change what the adapter does."

Why DEC-036 leaves this unchanged. The story's References cite DEC-035 (adopt conduct; one human end-gate; report organized by user-facing functionality; a legible auto-fix loop that records what it changed and dismissed) and DEC-036, which amends DEC-035's first binding decision by permitting a narrow, high-bar, stakes-gated mid-flight escalation tier (irreversible-and-imminent OR build-invalidating only), keeping routine findings always auto-fixed, requiring the report to render dismissals with a non-empty rationale, and adding the anti-rubber-stamp end-gate. The key design call recorded in the Dev Notes is that this story is unchanged by DEC-036: all three DEC-036 amendments are properties of the adapter and the Conductor, not of the quick-fix caller. By repointing at the adapter, quick-fix inherits them automatically — which is why the acceptance criteria encode them as pass-through obligations (don't gate routine findings, don't drop dismissals or their rationale, don't swallow or widen escalations) rather than as new behavior to build. The governing spec section is 12 (code-review adapter call-site wiring), of which this is the quick-fix caller repoint.

What changed in the diff below. The change is confined to the quick-fix call site. The primary edit is in `skills/momentum/skills/quick-fix/workflow.md`, at the code-review step, swapping the retired-stub reference for an invocation of the bmad-code-review adapter and removing any lingering mention of the old stub. The companion file `skills/momentum/skills/quick-fix/evals/eval-quickfix-code-review.md` was updated alongside it (the stale-sibling-eval correction flagged during review). Both files are listed as the item's deliverables.

The helpers, rescoped

Per-story quality checkingqa-reviewer-rescope-per-story-contract

This piece is the inspector that checks each story's work in isolation, before it merges: it runs that story's own tests (never just reads the code and assumes it works), marks every requirement as met, partly met, missing, or unverifiable, and — critically — labels each problem it finds with how dangerous it is (touches security, touches an irreversible operation like a database migration or deletion, or is ordinary). It gives conduct the guarantee that no story is declared "done" on the strength of source code that merely looks right, and that the build's later safety gates receive an honest danger signal stamped at the moment the problem is discovered. Without it, broken work could pass review unrun, and a serious problem sitting on a destructive operation could be mistaken for a harmless one and quietly auto-fixed.

Delivered: conduct now has a per-story reviewer that verifies one story's changes against that story's own contract by actually executing its tests, and stamps each finding with a danger class at the source — replacing the old whole-sprint, after-the-fact review.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. The frozen contract pinned down thirteen behaviors across six scenarios, all aimed at one shift: turn qa-reviewer from a whole-sprint, post-merge reviewer into a per-story, pre-merge one that also tags the stakes of every finding it produces. In plain terms, the rebuilt agent had to (1) review exactly one story's worktree diff against that one story's verification contract, and say nothing about any other story; (2) label every acceptance criterion as exactly one of VERIFIED, PARTIAL, MISSING, or BLOCKED, each backed by a concrete file-and-line citation pointing into the diff; (3) reach those verdicts by actually running the story's tests — reading source code and finding the right words in it is explicitly not allowed to stand in for executing a test; (4) keep MISSING (behavior genuinely absent) and BLOCKED (couldn't run the test at all) strictly separate; (5) start required services itself rather than declaring a service-backed criterion BLOCKED just because nothing was running yet; (6) run concurrently in stage 2 alongside the other reviewers, scoped to its single story; and (7-11) attach a `stakes_class` to every finding it emits, chosen from four defined classes by consulting the shared rubric — security/auth-isolation for security criteria, irreversible/destructive for a MISSING-or-PARTIAL finding sitting on a migration/delete/force-push/prod-deploy, high-blast-radius/architecture for cross-cutting interfaces, and routine as the fall-through default applied only when there is no real signal (never to mask one). Finally (12-13), all cross-story, sprint-wide, and integration reasoning had to leave qa-reviewer (it moves to AVFL), and the old post-merge-on-`main` whole-sprint path had to be gone.

How it was checked — and how strong that check is. A QA agent verified the rebuilt agent definition against the contract above, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for gaps; the item went through four rounds with a post-fix recheck on the finding shape. Be clear about what kind of verification this was: the deliverable is a single markdown agent-definition file (`skills/momentum/agents/qa-reviewer.md`), the verification method is `skill-invoke`, and the runtime harness for this sprint is currently inert. So "verified" here means structured inspection of the prose contract against the frozen acceptance criteria — not a live execution of qa-reviewer against a real story with real failing tests. Every required behavior is present and explicit in the file: per-story/one-worktree scoping with cross-story work routed to AVFL; the four-way classification with file:line evidence rules; an unusually firm statement that grep hits and source-reading are not evidence and that verdicts must rest on executed tests; a clean MISSING-vs-BLOCKED definition; a mandatory service-startup step that points at the project's e2e-validation rule; the stage-2 concurrency framing; and a stakes-class section that walks the four classes in priority order and names routine as the no-signal default. What was confirmed is that the instructions an invoked agent would follow match the contract — not that an actual invocation produced correctly-classified, correctly-tagged findings. That live behavioral check is deferred until the harness is active.

Result. Verdict: PASS, merged. The recorded evidence: four findings total — three fixed, one dismissed, none escalated, and the post-fix recheck came back REFIXED. The substantive fix during review was that the emitted-finding shape was reconciled with the canonical finding fields (the agent now carries a schema note explaining that an external normalization adapter, owned by the `directed-fix-finding-schema` story, adds `source`, `legitimate`, `severity`, `type`, `suggested_fix`, and `story_slug` downstream — so this producer's leaner output is intentional, not a gap). The one dismissed finding was cosmetic: the agent uses hyphenated class names (`security-auth-isolation`) where the story prose used slash-form (`security/auth-isolation`); the reviewer judged the values equivalent and dismissed it. The sole deliverable is `skills/momentum/agents/qa-reviewer.md`.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. Under the conduct rewrite, verification moves from "review the whole sprint after everything merges" to "verify each story in its own worktree, before it merges." This story rescopes qa-reviewer to match: its unit of work is now one story — that story's worktree diff, that story's contract, that story's tests — and it runs concurrently with the other stage-2 reviewers instead of waiting for a sprint-wide post-merge pass. Everything that requires seeing more than one story at once (integration between stories, sprint-wide consistency, contract drift) is deliberately removed from qa-reviewer and handed to AVFL, which runs once over the integrated result. This makes per-story verification legible and isolated: a finding always traces to exactly one story, and a reviewer can never produce a false statement about a story it wasn't given.

The second, equally deliberate choice is that stakes tagging lives at the producer. qa-reviewer is the agent that creates findings, and it is the only place in the pipeline that has both the acceptance-criterion text and the diff in view at once. That co-location is exactly what's needed to recognize, say, that a MISSING verdict is sitting on a database migration or a delete. If the stakes class were inferred later by a consumer that only sees finding prose, a structural false-negative becomes easy — a high-stakes finding gets silently treated as routine. So qa-reviewer sets `stakes_class` at the moment it writes each finding, by consulting the shared rubric, which keeps its tagging consistent with every other producer using that same rubric. Note the scope boundary the build respected: qa-reviewer only sets the *tag*; it does not decide the *disposition* (fixed/dismissed/escalated) or the timing tier — those are downstream concerns of the fix loop and the Conductor.

Why it's built this way — the governing decisions. This implements DEC-035 (adopt conduct: one human end-gate, no story-count cap, a legible auto-fix loop, replacing whole-sprint post-merge review with in-flight per-story verification) and DEC-036, which narrowly amends DEC-035 to add a stakes-gated escalation tier and an anti-rubber-stamp end-gate. The producer-side `stakes_class` exists specifically to feed two DEC-036 mechanisms: D2, the fixer-hold that pulls stakes-class findings out of the silent auto-fix path, and D4, the end-gate forcing function that surfaces them so the human reviewer can't rubber-stamp them. Both consume the signal this story produces. The governing conduct spec sections cited by the story are section 3, stage 2 (concurrent stage-2 reviewers — where qa-reviewer now runs, per story), section 4 (the build-phase verification structure the per-story review fits into), and section 10 (qa-reviewer's role and constraints within conduct). Two dependency stories shape the edges: `stakes-classification-rubric` defines the four classes and their signals (consumed here), and `directed-fix-finding-schema` owns the canonical finding shape that the tagged findings are normalized into downstream.

What changed in the diff below. A single file: `skills/momentum/agents/qa-reviewer.md` — a full rewrite of the agent definition from the whole-sprint post-merge model to the per-story, stage-2, stakes-tagging producer model. The three carried-forward "hard constraints" (execute tests / never substitute source-reading; MISSING-vs-BLOCKED; mandatory service startup) were preserved unchanged, since those are the reason qa-reviewer's verdicts are trusted at all.

What the builder is allowed to readdev-read-contract-part-a-header

This story teaches the automated coding agent to read the plain-language "acceptance target" written at the top of each work item's verification contract and to check its own work against that target before declaring the item finished — while strictly forbidding it from reading the full verifier (so it can't game the test) or from deciding how its work gets graded. Without this, the coder and the later grader could be aiming at different targets, letting work that misses the real acceptance bar slip through as "done."

Delivered: The coding agent now reads the contract's plain-language self-check header, confirms its work against it before signaling done, and records that the self-check happened — and it falls back cleanly to the story's written acceptance criteria when no such header exists.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. The frozen contract pinned this item to five observable behaviors, all about how the autonomous dev agent is allowed to touch a story's verification contract. First, before signaling it is done, the dev agent must read only the plain-language "Part A" header of the contract — the self-check prompt plus the observable clauses — treat those clauses as its acceptance target, and its completion signal must say the self-check was actually performed. Second, the dev agent must not author, edit, append to, or otherwise alter any part of the contract, and must not choose or change the verification method — it consumes the method as already given. Third, it must keep implementing against the story's full plain-English acceptance criteria (Part A is an extra safety check, not a substitute) and must never read the verifier body, "Part B." Fourth, this read behavior must stay identical no matter what stakes class or mid-flight escalation is active elsewhere in the flow — dev reads Part A, self-checks, signals done, and emits no stakes/disposition/escalation output of its own. Fifth, if a story has no readable Part-A header, the absence must not block completion — dev still builds against the ACs and signals done.

How it was checked. A QA agent verified the built files against those required behaviors clause by clause, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for ways the boundary could leak — for example, language that might let dev drift into the verifier body or silently edit the contract. High-risk findings were re-checked after fixes (the record shows a "REFIXED" recheck). Be clear about the strength of this verification: the deliverables are markdown agent and workflow definitions, and the runtime harness for this story is "skip," so nothing was executed live. Verification was by structured inspection against the frozen contract — reviewers reading the changed instructions and confirming they say what the contract requires — not by running a dev agent end to end and observing it obey. "Verified" here means the written rules match the required behavior, not that the behavior was demonstrated in a live run.

Result. The verdict is PASS, with the frozen contract confirmed unchanged ("freeze: MATCH"). Five findings were raised: four were fixed and one was dismissed, with zero escalated to stakes review. The concrete fixes recorded were a correction to the ExitWorktree timing around the self-check step, a spec cross-reference repair, and — notably — the Conductor reverting an out-of-scope edit the agent had made to a story file, keeping the change confined to its two intended deliverables. Both deliverables, `skills/momentum/agents/dev.md` and `skills/momentum/skills/dev/workflow.md`, were confirmed present and carrying the Part-A-only read allowance, the self-check step, the explicit no-authoring / no-method-choosing boundaries, the stakes-invariance note, and the missing-Part-A fallback. The item is merged.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. Before this change, the dev agent was forbidden from looking at any verification material at all — it built against the story's plain-English acceptance criteria and signaled done with no acceptance target it could read for itself. The conduct rewrite splits every story's verification contract into two parts: Part A, a deliberately plain-language header (a self-check prompt plus the observable clauses describing the acceptance target), and Part B, the verifier body that actually grades the work. This item removes the old "dev never sees the contract" rule and replaces it with a narrow, read-only allowance scoped to Part A only. The point is to let the autonomous builder aim at the same observable target the verifier will later check — closing the gap where dev and verifier could silently diverge — without ever letting dev author the contract, pick the verification method, or peek at the grader's body and thereby teach to the test. Part A becomes an extra self-check layer on top of the story's ACs, never a replacement for them.

Governing decisions and spec sections. This is grounded in DEC-035 (adopt the conduct execution engine: a single human end-gate and a legible build phase) and DEC-036 (the narrow, stakes-gated mid-flight escalation tier that amends DEC-035's first binding decision). The story's References and Dev Notes cite the conduct spec's section 7 Part A (the dev-readable verification header — the shape of what dev is now allowed to read) and section 10 (the dev-agent contract-consumption boundary — what dev must not reach). A deliberate design point, made explicit so it can be verified: this story inherits no behavioral change from DEC-036. DEC-036's stakes routing and escalation machinery govern how *findings* are dispositioned downstream; they never widen or narrow what the dev agent reads from the contract. That invariance is asserted directly in both changed files so the boundary cannot quietly erode later.

What changed in the diff below. Two files. `skills/momentum/agents/dev.md` — the dev agent definition — now states the Part-A-only read allowance, the no-authoring / no-method-choosing prohibitions, the stakes-invariance note, a green-field self-check step against the `how_dev_self_checks` prompt, and a `part_a_self_check` field ("performed" or "skipped-no-contract") in the structured completion signal. `skills/momentum/skills/dev/workflow.md` — the dev workflow — carries the matching critical rule and an explicit Step 2.5 that locates the contract, reads only the Part-A header, runs the self-check, and records whether it was performed or skipped before emitting the completion signal. The change is consume-only: emitting the contract is out of scope and lives in other conduct-core stories.

Taking git away from the builderdev-strip-merge-cleanup-authority

This story stripped the per-story developer agent of any power to merge code, clean up its own working directory, manage build locks, or ask a human how to recover from a crash — leaving it as a pure code-writer that only reports what it changed. The guarantee is that exactly one component (the Conductor) owns all git history changes and the single human approval gate; without it, every story-level agent could independently rewrite git history, leave stale locks behind, or pop up its own ad-hoc recovery prompts outside the one intended checkpoint.

Delivered: The developer agent now ends each run by reporting "implementation complete" plus the list of files it touched and nothing more, with all merge, worktree-cleanup, lock-handling, and crash-recovery authority removed and handed to the Conductor.
▶ Review this work item — testing first, then the architectural why, then the actual diff
① How it was verified (testing first)

What had to be true. The frozen contract held the `momentum:dev` agent to one narrow shape: after it finishes coding a story, it should report "implementation complete" plus the list of files it changed (the file_list) and then stop — nothing more. Concretely, six behaviors had to be gone from dev: (1) it must not propose a merge or pause waiting for merge approval; (2) it must not merge its branch into any target branch; (3) it must not resolve merge conflicts; (4) it must not remove, prune, or tear down a worktree; (5) it must not create, acquire, release, or clear any build/merge lock; and (6) on an interrupted or failed run it must not ask the human how to recover — it just stops. The contract also demanded an end-to-end attribution check: after a dev run, the only repository changes traceable to dev should be the implemented file edits — no merge commit, no torn-down worktree, no changed lock file. Finally, the old "no-auto-merge" critical rule had to be deleted outright (not merely reworded), since an agent that never touches merge has no reason to carry a rule forbidding auto-merge. The contract was deliberately black-box: every claim is meant to be observable from invoking dev and inspecting the repo afterward, not from reading dev's internals.

How it was checked. A QA agent verified the built item against those required behaviors, and an independent adversarial reviewer hunted for regressions; because this item carried real risk (a wrong edit could silently re-grant or mis-assign git authority), it was re-checked after fixes were applied. Be clear-eyed about the strength of that verification, though: the changed files are markdown agent and workflow definitions, and the runtime skill-invoke harness for this sprint was effectively in a "skip" posture — so verification was done by structured inspection of the published definitions against the frozen contract, not by actually executing `momentum:dev` and diffing a real repository. "Verified" here means a reviewer read the shipped dev agent and workflow and confirmed each required behavior is described as removed and reassigned to the Conductor — it does not mean the contract's scenarios were run live against an executing agent.

Result. The verdict is PASS, with the frozen contract recorded as MATCH (no scope drift between what was checked and what was frozen). Four findings surfaced during review; three were fixed and one was dismissed with rationale, none escalated. The most consequential catch is worth surfacing: the post-fix re-check found and corrected a regression in which the workflow had been edited to claim the Conductor handles a lock — a false assertion, since under a single Conductor the story-level lock simply no longer exists. That false lock-claim was removed so the shipped text says there is no lock to manage rather than implying the Conductor manages one. The deliverable was merged via a semantic conflict resolution on `dev/workflow.md` that preserved both editing intents. Inspecting the merged files confirms the end state: dev's terminal contract is "implementation complete" + file_list, and both files explicitly disclaim worktree management, merge/rebase/conflict resolution, lockfile handling, and crash-recovery prompts, assigning each to the Conductor.

② Why it's built this way — architecture & decision references

The architectural choice. This story shrinks `momentum:dev` from a near-end-to-end story agent into a pure implementer. Previously every per-story dev agent could merge its own work, resolve conflicts, clean up its worktree, juggle a lockfile, and — on a crash — ask the human ad-hoc how to recover. That scattered git-mutation authority across every story-level agent, which meant the git history could diverge in many places, locks could be left stale, and humans could be pulled into recovery questions outside any single, predictable gate. The new design consolidates all of that into one owner, the Conductor: dev produces the change, reports what it touched, and stops. There is now exactly one place that owns the build phase, the git history, the worktree lifecycle, and the single human end-gate.

Why it was made — governing decisions. This consolidation is the foundation of the conduct rewrite, which replaces `momentum:sprint-dev` with an in-session, per-story, autonomous build behind a single human gate. Per DEC-035, the Conductor owns the build phase and all git mutation, with one human gate at the end — which is incoherent as long as dev can merge on its own. DEC-036 (D1) makes the dependency explicit: relocating git-mutation authority out of dev and into the Conductor is a stated precondition for the Conductor to own the narrow, stakes-gated mid-flight escalation tier. Only when dev can no longer merge or otherwise mutate git can the Conductor be the single point that decides whether an irreversible operation pauses the run mid-flight or waits for the end-gate. The story's References and Dev Notes cite the conduct spec sections that drove the specific removals: section 3 (build-phase ownership moves to the Conductor; dev is a per-story implementer only), section 6 (git mutation, merge, and conflict resolution belong to the Conductor), and section 12 (worktree lifecycle, lockfile handling, and crash recovery belong to the Conductor). The "no-auto-merge" critical rule was deleted rather than retained because, once dev never touches merge at all, a rule forbidding it to auto-merge is moot.

What changed in the diff below. Three files: `skills/momentum/agents/dev.md` (the agent definition — scope line, output schema, and the "what dev does not do" rules rewritten to disclaim merge/worktree/lock/recovery and point to the Conductor; the no-auto-merge rule removed), `skills/momentum/skills/dev/workflow.md` (the workflow now ends at implementation-complete + file_list with no merge/worktree/lock/crash-ask steps, and a note reassigning those authorities to the Conductor), and `skills/momentum/skills/dev/SKILL.md` (the dev skill surface kept consistent with the narrowed contract).

All subsequent steps operate on the main tree.</action>--    <note>bmad-dev-story handles: story loading, sprint tracking, review continuation detection, task implementation loop, definition-of-done gate, story transition to review status. The Momentum Implementation Guide in the story tells it to use EDD for skill-instruction tasks rather than TDD.</note>-    <note>bmad-dev-story runs inside the worktree — all its file writes land in `.worktrees/story-{{story_key}}/`, isolated from other sessions.</note>-    <note>The Part-A self-check runs inside the worktree context (before ExitWorktree) so it inspects the just-built implementation, not the unmodified main tree.</note>+    <note>No EnterWorktree/ExitWorktree is needed — the Conductor already scoped this agent to the story worktree. This ensures the story branch includes all recent main changes (e.g., status updates from other merged stories) and conflicts are resolved before the merge.</action>-      <check if="rebase reports conflicts">-        <output>Rebase conflicts detected on story/{{story_key}}.
